==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The future electric power system will be less dependent or even independent of fossil
fuels, implying the generation primarily will be based on renewable energy sources
(RES), such as wind and solar. However, the usage of RES pose a great challenge
with respect to a secure and reliable operation of the electric grid, since the power
generation will have a variable output and be dependent on environmental conditions.
Therefore, other technologies such as energy storage and demand response have to be
utilized to ensure the balance of generation and consumption at all times. However,
this results in the generation and consumption pattern frequently changing, which
may lead to a fluctuating operation point in the electric power system. Consequently,
today’s stability and security tools, which are generally dependent on offline studies,
may no longer be suﬀicient. Hence, ongoing research is focusing on other alternatives,
such as performing these assessments in real-time based on wide-area measurements
obtained utilizing the phasor measurement technology.
This thesis concerns the implementation and validation of a method used to as-
sess transient voltage sags occurring due to large generator rotor swings following a
transient disturbance in an electric power system. The method has been extended
to assess a power system in real-time based on its current operating point, and a
screening mechanism was developed in order to screen through a list of plausible
contingencies. In order to perform the transient voltage sag assessment (TVSA), the
system has to remain transient stable after the transient disturbance, which is deter-
mined using the SIngle Machine Equivalent (SIME) method. SIME is able to perform
a first-swing transient stability assessment (TSA), and is well suited in a real-time
application. Furthermore, load voltage sensitivities are used to identify the critical
generator, which for a change in its rotor angle, contributes with the largest depres-
sion of the voltage sag. The real-time implementation has been achieved by coupling
the method with the Wide-Area Real-Time Application (WARTApp) platform, which
provides the method with phasor measurement unit (PMU) snapshots. Based on each
PMU snapshot the method is able to automatically pin-point critical contingencies,
their location, the severity of the transient voltage sag, and the contribution of each
generator to the transient voltage sag, from where it highlights the critical generator.
For a given PMU snapshot, the method is able to screen through a list of 51 con-
tingencies in 37 s. Furthermore, preventive control actions were investigated, and by
reducing the pre-fault active power output of the critical generator and redispatching
this amount to non-critical generators proved to reduce the severity of the transient
voltage sag. In addition, information obtained from the TSA and TVSA is visualized
in a table where a color scheme is used, which improves the grid operator’s system
awareness. Further, critical contingencies are highlighted, enabling the grid operator
to focus more time and effort on investigating these in detail.

Preface
This thesis was prepared at the Department of Electrical Engineering at the Technical
University of Denmark with support from Danish Energy. The workload of the project
has been set to 35 ECTS points.
The study started on the 1st of February 2021 and was completed on the 1st of August
2021.
The thesis has been prepared under the supervision of Hjörtur Jóhannsson and Tilman
Weckesser.
Jacob Bollerslev
Kongens Lyngby, August 2021

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
The modern society is very dependent on the availability of electric energy.
From 2004
to 2014 the world-wide electricity consumption increased with 21 %, whereas the access to
electricity increased with almost 7 % in the same period.
Since 2014 many more people
have gained access to electricity, and in 2019, 90 % of the world-wide population had access
to electricity. This is an increase of 5 % since 2014 [1]. This shows that society’s depen-
dency on a reliable and secure electric power system is increasing. In 2003 a blackout in the
northeast of the United States of America and in Ontario, Canada occurred, which affected
approximately 50 million people and lasted up to four days. This was estimated to have
caused a loss of $4 billion to $10 billion (U.S. dollars) [2]. After the blackout a task force
was set to investigate the blackout. They concluded that one of the key causes was the lack
of situational awareness, due to the tools provided to the grid operator were insuﬀicient.
Therefore, it was recommended to investigate real-time operating tools, in order to enhance
the reliability and security of the power system. The Paris agreement, signed by 195 coun-
tries agreed to limit the average global temperature increase to less than 2◦C this century,
however, this requires a significant reduction in the greenhouse gas emissions [3]. According
to [4] an analysis shows that by 2050 the CO2 emissions in EU can be fully eliminated
from the power sector by shifting from conventional to renewable energy sources (RES),
such as wind and solar. However, [4] states that the usage of RES pose a great challenge
with respect to secure and reliable operation of the electric grid, since the power generation
based on RES have a variable output and is dependent on the environmental conditions.
Therefore, technologies such as energy storage and demand response have to be utilized to
ensure the balance of generation and consumption at all times. However, this results in the
generation and consumption pattern frequently changing, which may lead to a fluctuating
operating point in the power system. Presently, the tools and methods used to monitor and
assess stability and security of the power system are based on extensive offline studies and
are performed in advance, e.g., a day ahead. This approach is suﬀicient in a system where
generation is based on conventional energy sources, since the consumption pattern is well
defined by the transmission system operator (TSO), implying the system operating point is
highly predictable. However, these stability and security assessment methods may no longer
be suﬀicient with fluctuating energy sources, since the consumption pattern frequently has
to be adjusted to match the generation, resulting in a fluctuating operating point, which is
diﬀicult to predict. An alternative to the offline stability and security assessments is to as-
sess the system in real-time based on wide-area measurements obtained utilizing the phasor
measurement technology [5, 6, 7]. According to [8, 9] the phasor measurement technology
allows for the development of real-time wide-area monitoring and control applications. The
Danish research project Secure Operation of Sustainable Power Systems (SOSPO) developed
real-time wide-area stability and security assessment methods by utilizing phasor measure-
ment units (PMUs) [10]. Further, these methods improve the system awareness of the TSO
by visualizing the assessment and provide countermeasures in case of a disturbance.
==PAGE:16==
1.2 State-of-the-art
2
1.2
State-of-the-art
SOSPO was a project initiated in 2012, with the focus on critical, diﬀicult and not yet treated
problems regarding how secure operation of future sustainable power systems can be ensured
[10]. The project developed new stability assessment methods, which are designed to work
in real-time to automatically detect critical contingencies and determine appropriate control
actions to regain secure operation of the power system, which is coveted by TSOs. This will
enable the operators to obtain a better observability in the power system, and maintain
secure operation for situations which otherwise could result in unstable operation and poten-
tially a blackout. Thus, in order to test these methods and visualize their assessment and
control actions in real-time a closed-loop SOSPO simulation platform (SOSPO-SP) was de-
veloped [11]. SOSPO-SP also known as WARTApp (Wide-Area Real-Time Application) [12]
can either run a preloaded time-domain simulation (TDS) created with Siemens Power Sys-
tem Simulator for Engineering (PSS/E), or connect to a real time digital simulator (RTDS).
The RTDS depends on external hardware and allows true closed-loop control compared to
the preloaded simulations based on PSS/E, which can be run on a single computer, e.g., a
laptop. This means that it is only possible to see the effect of control actions derived from
the method in real-time when the RTDS is used. However, the preloaded PSS/E simulation
is well suited for testing and validating a method, and is independent of external hardware
[11, p. 2320-2322]. If these methods are deployed in a real power system, they will rely on
PMUs which are able to provide more information and enables wide-area monitoring of the
power system. A PMU signal represents a time-domain signal by its magnitude and phase,
and the Global Positioning System (GPS) is used to synchronize the PMU signals with re-
spect to time. Examples of PMU signals are complex bus voltages, generators currents and
branch currents. The WARTApp platform emulates the real power system by sending PMU
signals to the methods, which implies that these methods easily can be utilized in the real
power system, given the PMU infrastructure is established. From the SOSPO project this
thesis focuses on the method assessing the occurrence of transient voltage sags due to large
generator rotor swings, as presented in the following.
According to Weckesser et al. the occurrence of voltage sags at load buses caused by
large generator rotor swings are rarely addressed [13]. Certain contingencies, such as trans-
mission line faults can cause large generator rotor displacements, which result in transiently
low voltages. These contingencies may be assessed transient stable, since the generators
remain in synchronism. However, due to the large generator rotor angle displacements, bus
voltages in the post-fault configuration after fault clearance may reduce below a critical level
which may cause cascading effects, such as load tripping which may further deteriorate the
condition of the system. These large rotor angle displacements occur due to one or more
generators accelerating relative to the other generators during the fault. An example of this
is seen in Fig. 1.1 where a three-phase short-circuit is applied on a transmission line at t = 1 s
connecting buses 16 and 21 in the New England & New York system and cleared after 150
ms.
==PAGE:17==
1.2 State-of-the-art
3
Figure 1.1: Evolution of generator rotor angles (with respect to center of inertia) [13].
Weckesser et al. observed large rotor swings for GEN-6 and GEN-7, which both reach a
return angle after fault clearance, implying the system is first-swing transient stable. How-
ever, as seen in Fig. 1.2 buses 15, 16, 21, 23 and 24 experience a transient voltage sag, which
for some of them drop below the critical value of 0.70 pu [14].
Figure 1.2: Evolution of voltage magnitudes at load buses [13].
Different methods for assessing transient voltage sags at load buses caused by generator
rotor swings were investigated in [13]. Three different prediction methods were proposed in
order to predict the minimum of the transient voltage sag. These methods rely on real-time
measurements from PMUs, and assess the power system in the post-fault configuration, i.e.,
after fault clearance.
The first method relies on acquiring PMU voltage measurements in the post-fault con-
figuration, where a quadratic approximation is used to predict the voltage evolution at each
==PAGE:18==
1.2 State-of-the-art
4
load bus. The quadratic approximation is improved using a recursive least square (RLS)
estimator together with new measurements at each step.
The second method relies on predicting the evolution of each generator’s rotor angle.
A quadratic approximation of the rotor angle evolution is used and the approximation is
improved using a RLS estimator. Thus, each generator is represented by a classical transient
stability model, since the phase of the internal complex voltage can be represented directly by
the rotor angle of the generator. The generator’s stator is represented by a Norton equivalent,
which by shifting the internal complex voltage with a predicted change in the rotor angle,
and solving ¯I = Y ¯V for ¯V allows the prediction of the minimum voltage magnitude at the
load buses during the transient voltage sag. ¯I contains the complex current injection into
each bus, which is only non-zero for generator buses, since loads are converted to constant
shunt admittances. Y the admittance matrix of the power system and ¯V the complex bus
voltages.
The third method utilize the Emergency SIngle Machine Equivalent (E-SIME) method to
predict the minimum bus voltage magnitudes. Fig. 1.3 illustrates the earliness and accuracy
of each method at predicting the minimum voltage magnitude occurring in the TDS indicated
by the red square. Weckesser et al. determined that both the second and third method were
able to allow an early detection of critical buses and voltage sag minimums. However, they
concluded that the predictive method employing E-SIME was the most promising of the
three methods, since it allows for an early and consistent identification of critical load buses,
together with an accurate prediction of the voltage sag minimum. Further, they concluded
that its computation time is acceptable, and is suitable for a real-time application [13].
Figure 1.3: Prediction of voltage minimum at bus 24 (load 14) occurring at the time indicated by
the red square [13].
The SIngle Machine Equivalent (SIME) method was designed in order to assess first-
swing transient stability of a multi-machine system [15]. SIME is based on the equal area
criterion (EAC), which is a direct method and is applicable for a two-machine system. How-
ever, normally the machine of concern is connected to an infinite bus, in order to assess the
transient stability of the machine, which is referred to as a one machine infinite bus (OMIB)
==PAGE:19==
1.2 State-of-the-art
5
equivalent. The infinite bus represents a machine with infinite inertia and zero impedance.
It is a source with constant voltage (both in phase and magnitude) and frequency. There-
fore, an important task of the SIME method is to ensure that the single machine in the
OMIB equivalent captures the dominant dynamics of the multi-machine system, in order to
properly assess the transient stability of the multi-machine system. The E-SIME method is
based on the SIME concept, and has been extended to enable its use for real-time assessment
of the power system, and is used to control the system after clearance of a contingency [15,
p. 173][16]. However, the E-SIME method can also be used to predict whether any possible
contingencies will become first-swing transient unstable. The acquired PMU signals from
the power system are used to initialize a TDS, and together with E-SIME it is possible to
predict if any contingency that may occur in the real power system will cause transient insta-
bility [15, p. 176]. Thus, appropriate control actions can be provided by the method before
such a contingency might occur. The third method proposed by Weckesser et al. uses the
predictive E-SIME method, which firstly determines if a contingency is first-swing transient
stable, and if yes, then predicts the occurrence of any transient voltage sag.
In [17] Weckesser et al. extended the work to pin-point which of the generators were con-
tributing negatively to the transient voltage sag, in order to propose appropriate preventive
control actions. This work proposed the usage of a sensitivity matrix, which allows iden-
tifying the generators having a dominant impact on a certain load bus voltage magnitude.
The sensitivity matrix has units of pu/rad or V/rad. Each entry in the sensitivity matrix
informs how much the voltage magnitude at a given load bus changes per 1 rad change of a
given generator’s rotor angle. The entries can be positive or negative, and the sign indicates
whether increasing the rotor angle will increase or depress the voltage magnitude.
The work performed by Weckesser et al. is innovative and novel since there is no previous
work in the literature which investigates transient voltage sags caused by angular separation
of generators using the system dynamics as presented in [13, 17]. Furthermore, the work
is relevant for the future power system, since it is able to be implemented in a real-time
application and predict the occurrence of transient voltage sags. Currently, in the literature
on power quality, voltage sags is a topic vastly covered [18, 19]. There is also some work in
the literature addressing the occurrence of transient voltage sags due to angular separation
of generators [20, 21]. In [20] the direct method, transient energy function (TEF) is utilized
to calculate the minimum of transient voltage sags caused by large rotor swings. Whereas,
in [21] a hybrid method of TEF is utilized which combines TEF with a TDS. The hybrid
method was used to perform a sensitivity analysis to relate the voltage sag depths to certain
parameters, such as terminal voltages and power generation. Although, according to [13]
TEF suffers from restrictions placed on the power system model and according to [22, p.
954] the best results are obtained utilizing the hybrid method due to limitations of the
TEF method.
However, since the publication of this work in the 1990s technology has
greatly advanced, reducing the computational effort required to perform a TDS, which newer
methods, such as SIME has taking advantage of, improving the accuracy of the method’s
assessment [15, p. 17,190].
==PAGE:20==
1.3 Research description
6
1.3
Research description
This thesis aims to implement and validate a method, which can assess the transient voltage
sags occurring due to large generator rotor swings, following a transient disturbance in
electric power systems. The original method was proposed in Early Prediction of Transient
Voltage Sags caused by Rotor Swings by Weckesser et al. where this project extends the
contribution from this work.
The focus is on implementation of the method in Python
utilizing the WARTApp platform, which provides the framework for extensive validation of
the method. The method will be further extended by developing a screening mechanism to
automatically pin-point critical contingencies and locations in the power system.
1.4
Research objectives
The primary research question this thesis seeks to answer is: How can the occurrence of
transient voltage sags be automatically predicted, and their location pin-pointed
for various contingencies in a power system?
The primary research question can be split into the following sub-research questions:
• How can transient stability of a power system with multiple generators be assessed,
and if transient stable how can a transient voltage sag be predicted?
The E-SIME method will be used to construct an OMIB equivalent, in order to ap-
ply the EAC to determine if the power system is first swing transient stable. For
any system, which is first swing transient stable, it will be possible to predict the
minimum value of any transient voltage sags occurring. This approach is based on
Early Prediction of Transient Voltage Sags caused by Rotor Swings by Weckesser et al.
• How can a screening mechanism be developed, which automatically pin-points critical
contingencies, their location and the contribution of each generator to the transient
voltage sag in the power system?
The method will screen through each contingency, determine the system’s first-swing
transient stability using E-SIME, and for the first-swing transient stable ones, pre-
dict the minimum of the transient voltage sag. In addition, the method proposed in
Derivation and Application of Sensitivities to Assess Transient Voltage Sags caused
by Rotor Swings by Weckesser et al. will be used to determine the contribution of
each generator to the transient voltage sag. This will make it possible to pin-point
which generators are contributing negatively to the transient voltage sag.
• How can this screening mechanism be used to monitor a power system in real-time?
In order to apply the screening mechanism in real-time, the current state of the power
system has to be extracted using PMU signals. These PMU signals are used to ini-
tialize the TDS tool, which the screening mechanism relies upon. This ensures that it
is possible to predict the occurrence of any transient voltage sag ahead of time based
on the current state of the power system.
==PAGE:21==
1.4 Research objectives
7
• What type of preventive control action can be performed in order to prevent the occur-
rence of the predicted transient voltage sag, in case the critical contingency occurs in
the power system?
The type of preventive control action which can prevent the occurrence of the tran-
sient voltage sag will be investigated, by utilizing a case where a critical transient
voltage sag has been identified. This will give the grid operator insight into which
type of preventive control action to perform, in order to prevent the occurrence of any
transient voltage sag possibly occurring in the future after a transient disturbance.
• How can an operator of the power system be alerted by these predicted critical contin-
gencies in order to ensure system awareness?
An overview of the entire power system needs to be provided, in order to relay this
information to the operator. This information needs to be clear and concise, meaning
it needs to alert the operator of the following for each contingency: Severity of the
predicted transient voltage sag, location in the power system, and which generator is
contributing most negatively to the transient voltage sag.
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 2
Theory
In this chapter the theory used for the implementation of E-SIME, prediction of transient
voltage sags and the recursive least square estimator is described.
2.1
SIME
SIME is a transient stability method which is used to assess first-swing transient stability
of a multi-machine system and is based on the generalized one-machine infinite bus (OMIB)
equivalent.
SIME compresses multimachine data to assess the transient stability of the
multimachine system and extract the critical machines, which are responsible for the loss
of synchronism. The method is a hybrid, temporal-direct method. The method is temporal
since it relies on the evolution of the multimachine system with respect to time and direct
since it relies on the extended equal area criterion (EEAC) from where it originates [15, p.
35]. EEAC is a pure direct method, implying it does not depend on a TDS. EEAC relies
on a time-invariant OMIB, which is constructed from all the machines in the multimachine
system once and for all at the initial time of the disturbance.
The construction of the
OMIB is based on the fact that once a system becomes transient unstable, one or more
generators loses synchronism, which causes the multimachine system to separate into two
groups. Hence, EEAC is based on subdividing the system machines into two groups: the
critical group and the non-critical group. The critical group is composed of the machines
which are responsible for the loss of synchronism, i.e., the machines which have accelerated
relative to the other machines during the fault. The non-critical group is composed of the
rest of the machines. The two groups are used to construct an equivalent machine describing
the dynamics of the multimachine system, which is the machine used in the OMIB equivalent.
Hence, by applying the EAC to the OMIB it is possible to assess the transient stability of
the multimachine system.
SIME concentrates on the post-fault configuration of the system and uses a time-varying
OMIB, which means that the parameters of the OMIB are updated using the multimachine
temporal data. SIME searches for an OMIB candidate which represents the actual dynamical
behavior of the multimachine system, by identifying several OMIB candidates, consisting of
a different number of critical machines (CMs) and non-critical machines (NMs). The first-
swing transient stability assessment is performed for each time step of the TDS, since the
parameters of each identified OMIB are updated for each time step. The candidate which
may become first-swing transient unstable is considered the critical candidate.
The dynamical behavior of the OMIB equivalent is described using the classical generator
model. The motion of the rotor is described by a second-order differential equation, however,
it can be represented by two first-order differential equations. In the following these two
first-order differential equations are provided in pu where (2.1) describes the change in rotor
speed based on the mismatch between the mechanical torque Tm and the electrical torque
Te. Whereas, (2.2) describes the change in the machine’s rotor angle based on the mismatch
between the machine’s rotor speed ω and the power system’s synchronous speed ω0.
In
==PAGE:23==
2.1 SIME
9
addition, the classical generator model assumes the internal voltage magnitude behind the
transient reactance to be constant. Further, it is assumed that the rotor speed will change by
a negligible amount compared to synchronous speed, meaning it can be considered constant
or close to 1 pu. This implies that in pu then Pm = Tm and Pe = Te [22, p. 829]. However,
it is important to stress that from a physical point of view these powers are torques acting
on the machine’s rotor.

frequency in Hz of the electric field on the stator side [22, p. 51-52].
The OMIB used in SIME does not consider any load damping and has not been included
in (2.1) [15, p. 208].
2.1.1
OMIB time-varying parameters
The OMIB equivalent is obtained from the decomposition of the machines in the multima-
chine system into CMs and NMs. The machines within each group are aggregated to form
an equivalent machine describing the dynamics of the CMs and NMs, respectively. These
two machines are replaced with an equivalent machine, which is connected to an infinite
bus, forming the OMIB equivalent. Thus, the machine included in the OMIB equivalent de-
scribes the dynamical behavior of all the machines in the multimachine system. The OMIB
parameters δ, ω, M, Pm, Pe, Pa are derived at each time step of the TDS for the given OMIB
using the newly acquired multimachine temporal data from the TDS [15, p. 40-41].
The center of inertia (COI) is computed for each group, where C and N is the subscript
for CMs and NMs, respectively.

2.1.2
Equal area criterion and stability margin
EAC relies on the concept of energy and is used to assess if the kinetic energy the machine
has gained in the during-fault configuration, can be dissipated in the post-fault configuration
before the machine’s rotor angle crosses the unstable angle δu and loses synchronism. The
kinetic energy gained is represented by the area Aacc, and the total area where the machine
can decelerate and dissipate the gained kinetic energy is represented by the area Adec. This
allows for the stability margin η to be established as given in (2.17) [15, p. 41]. The reason
the machine either accelerates or decelerates is due to the mismatch between the electrical
power Pe and the mechanical power Pm of the machine as seen from (2.1). Pe is the electrical
==PAGE:25==
2.1 SIME
11
active power output of the machine, and Pm is the mechanical power used to rotate the rotor.
The mismatch between these two is referred to as the accelerating power Pa = Pm −Pe.
Thus, when Pa > 0 the machine accelerates, due to Pm > Pe, and when Pa < 0 the machine
decelerates, due to Pe > Pm. Note, when dealing with EAC the classical generator model is
typically used, implying in pu powers and torques are used interchangeably [22, p. 827].
η = Adec −Aacc,
(2.17)
where the machine is transient stable if η > 0 and unstable if η < 0. If η = 0 it becomes
stable when reaching the unstable angle δu.
More specifically (2.17) can be given by (2.18), which describes the concept of EAC
mathematically and expresses the stability margin in terms of the during-fault and post-
fault configurations. However, the stability margin can be represented by a more convenient
expression with the E-SIME method as presented in section 2.1.5. E-SIME is able to calculate
the stability margin by focusing on the post-fault configuration, and is designed with the
intent for usage in real-time applications.
η = −
Z δch
δ0
Padδ −
Z δu
δch
Padδ.
(2.18)
The angle δ0 denotes the initial rotor angle of the machine when the fault occurs. Angle
δch denotes the angle where Pa changes sign from positive to negative. Typically, once the
fault is cleared the machine’s Pa changes instantly from positive to negative, which means
that the clearing angle δe = δch, however, this might not always be the case [15, p. 42]. This
means that if δe < δch, the machine will continue to accelerate in the post-fault configuration,
and first when the rotor angle passes δch will Pa < 0.
An example of a transient stable case is seen in Fig. 2.1. Applying (2.18) to this example
would result in η > 0, implying the machine has dissipated the kinetic energy gained in
the during-fault configuration, which means the rotor speed has decelerated and reached
synchronous speed while Pa < 0. The angle reached by the machine when this occurs is
referred to as the return angle δr and δr < δu. Due to Pa < 0 the rotor speed reduces below
synchronous speed, causing the rotor angle to decrease, which is in accordance with (2.2).
In Fig. 2.1 the return angle is reached at δr = 146.7◦, from where the rotor angle decreases
until Pe = Pm is reached at a rotor angle which satisfies this. As seen Pe oscillates around
the rotor angle where Pe = Pm, and it will oscillate around this rotor angle indefinitely
unless damping is included in the generator model. This oscillatory behavior occurs due
to the inertia of the rotor preventing the rotor angle from changing instantaneously. Thus,
once Pe = Pm is reached then due to ω < ω0 the rotor angle continues to decrease, causing
Pm > Pe. This results in an acceleration, and once Pe = Pm is reached again, then ω > ω0,
resulting in a continued increase in the rotor angle, causing Pe > Pm, which results in a
deceleration [22, p. 833-834]. This is exactly the oscillatory behavior seen in Fig. 2.1.

δ0 = 55.3◦,
δe = 65.1◦, δch = 92.2◦, δr = 146.7◦[15]. Adec only represents the area used to dissipate the gained
kinetic energy from the during-fault configuration, and is not be confused with Adec in (2.17), which
represents the total deceleration area available from δch to δu.
2.1.3
Stability conditions
The stability conditions for an OMIB candidate with time-varying parameters are provided
in the following [15, p. 43-44].
Conditions of an unstable OMIB candidate
An unstable OMIB corresponds to η < 0, which corresponds to Adec < Aacc. This implies
that the machine is unable to dissipate the gained kinetic energy from the during-fault
configuration in the post-fault configuration. This causes Pe to cross Pm at δ = δu, resulting
in Pa > 0 for δ > δu. This implies the rotor starts to accelerate, which results in a loss
of synchronism. Thus, an OMIB has reached the unstable angle δu once the following is
satisfied:
Pa(δu) = 0 and ˙Pa(δu) > 0.
(2.19)
If the rotor speed of the machine is above synchronous speed when (2.19) is satisfied,
i.e., when δu is reached, then the rotor angle will continue to increase causing δ > δu. This
causes Pa > 0, which results in the machine losing synchronism. In section 2.1.5 (2.19) is
used as a condition to check that the obtained Pa −δ curve fit is physically correct in order
to correctly calculate the stability margin.
Conditions of a stable OMIB candidate
A stable OMIB corresponds to η > 0, which corresponds to Adec > Aacc. This implies that
the machine is able to dissipate the gained kinetic energy from the during-fault configuration
==PAGE:27==
2.1 SIME
13
in the post-fault configuration. The machine’s rotor speed will reach synchronous speed at
δ = δr where Pa < 0. From this point the rotor angle will start to decrease, since the rotor
speed reduces below synchronous speed due to Pa < 0. Thus, an OMIB has reached its
return angle δr (δr < δu) once the following is satisfied:
ω(δr) = 0 and Pa(δr) < 0.
(2.20)
As mentioned in section 2.1.1 the synchronous rotor speed of the OMIB is zero, hence
the rotor speed is equal to zero when reaching the return angle.
Borderline stability condition for an OMIB candidate
When η = 0, then Adec = Aacc, which implies that the machine reaches its return angle at
the unstable angle. This implies that when δr = δu then Pa(δr) = 0 and ω(δr) = 0, which
coincides with the unstable condition for the OMIB candidate (2.19). However, despite the
system being transient stable when δr = δu, it cannot be considered a safe operating point,
since for a small disturbance the generator would lose synchronism.
2.1.4
Identification of critical machines when constructing OMIB
candidates
SIME starts identifying CMs when the TDS enters the post-fault configuration by consid-
ering each time step of the simulation. SIME sorts the generators according to their rotor
angles in a descending order, and calculates the angular deviation between two adjacent
generators. The generators above the largest angular deviation form the group of CMs and
the ones below form the group of NMs. This can be illustrated by considering the follow-
ing example. Assume a total of 5 generators and their rotor angles have been sorted in a
descending order in the following:
δsorted =
h
δG5 δG3 δG4 δG1 δG2
iT
,
(2.21)
where δG5 > δG3 > δG4 > δG1 > δG2.
Assume the largest angular deviation is obtained between the two adjacent generators,
G3 and G4:
∆δmax = δG3 −δG4,
(2.22)
where ∆δmax is the maximum angular deviation and marks the crossover between the group
of CMs and NMs. Hence, the group of CMs consists of G3, G5, whereas, the group of NMs
consists of G4, G1, G2.
Once the group of CMs and NMs have been identified the parameters for the OMIB
candidate can be obtained according to to section 2.1.1. Each identified OMIB candidate
is unique and is represented by its group of CMs and NMs. In order to identify several
candidates for the same time step, the angular deviation such as the second and third
largest can be considered alongside the largest angular deviation.
2.1.5
E-SIME
E-SIME was originally designed to monitor and trigger control actions in real-time after a
harmful contingency had occurred in the power system, without the need for performing a
==PAGE:28==
2.1 SIME
14
TDS. The measurements needed are machine rotor angles, rotor speeds and accelerations,
which are acquired from main power plants [15, p. 173-174]. However, an alternative to
emergency SIME is predictive SIME, which is able to predict harmful contingencies before
they potentially occur in the power system using a TDS. Predictive SIME also relies on
real-time measurements, however, it requires wide-area data such as complex bus voltages,
branch currents and generator currents obtained from PMUs in order to perform an accurate
TDS which reflects the real power system. In order to perform the assessment, predictive
SIME require the following measurements from each machine: rotor angle, rotor speed,
mechanical and electrical power. This is obtained using the multimachine temporal data
available during the TDS [15, p. 176]. Except from these differences emergency SIME and
predictive SIME share the same assessment method of the system’s transient stability. Also,
Weckesser et al. refers to predictive SIME as E-SIME, and to be consistent with existing
literature predictive SIME is also referred to as E-SIME in this thesis. In addition, both
of these methods are considered closed-loop methods, since they are able to monitor and
control the system in real-time, and assess whether the applied control actions are suﬀicient
or should be reinforced [15, p. 172]. The following presents the assessment method. However,
before presenting the procedure, the stability margin is presented in the following:
η = −
Z δu
δi
Pa(δ)dδ −1
2Mω2
i ,
(2.23)
where η is given in rad. δi and ωi is the rotor angle in rad and rotor speed in pu of the
OMIB at time ti, respectively. Pa is the accelerating power in pu of the OMIB during the
post-fault configuration, and the integral represents the total remaining decelerating area
from the current rotor angle δi up to the unstable angle δu. The second term is the kinetic
energy of the OMIB at time ti. Since η > 0 for a stable case and η < 0 for an unstable case,
then while Pa > 0 the contribution must be negative and while Pa < 0 the contribution must
be positive from the integral, hence the reason for the negative sign in front of the integral.
The Pa−δ curve of the OMIB, which is used in (2.23) can be approximated by a quadratic
curve, which is deemed suﬀicient according to [15, p. 177]:
Pa (δ) = aδ2 + bδ + c,
(2.24)
where the parameters a, b and c are estimated using the first three successive measurements
of Pa and δ in the post-fault configuration. Subsequently, the estimate of the Pa −δ curve is
refined using new measurements of Pa and δ by applying a weighted RLS technique presented
in section 2.3. However, in order to ensure that the stability margin is correctly calculated,
the approximated Pa −δ curve from (2.24) must satisfy the instability condition (2.19) when
δ = δu. If this condition is not satisfied then the obtained Pa −δ curve is not physically
correct, and the calculation of the stability margin is skipped. This check is made for every
time step after the parameters of the Pa −δ curve have been refined.
Further, when identifying the CMs and NMs to form candidate OMIBs then the evolu-
tion of the rotor angle for each generator is predicted ahead of time, to allow for a clearer
separation between the CMs and NMs. According to Weckesser et al. a quadratic approx-
imation of the rotor angle’s evolution is suﬀicient. Thus, the rotor angle evolution of the
k-th generator is obtained as:
δk(t) = αkt2βkt + γk,
(2.25)
where the parameters αk, βk and γk are estimated using the first three successive measure-
ments of δk at times t = [ti −2∆t, ti −∆t, ti] in the post-fault configuration. Subsequently,
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Transient voltage sags due to rotor swings
15
the estimate of the k-th generator’s rotor angle is refined by applying the weighted RLS
technique presented in section 2.3.
Finally, the procedure for applying the E-SIME method is presented in the following:
1. Collect the first three successive measurements at times ti −2∆t, ti −∆t, ti of each
generator’s rotor angle, rotor speed, mechanical and electrical power in the post-fault
configuration. Consider the measurements obtained at time ti in the following steps
until step 8 is reached.
2. For each generator, using (2.25) predict the rotor angle some time ahead, e.g., 100 ms.
3. The predicted rotor angles are used to identify the CMs and NMs as described in
section 2.1.4.
4. The parameters for the candidate OMIB is determined in accordance with section
2.1.1.
5. The OMIB parameters, Pa and δ are utilized to estimate its Pa −δ curve with (2.24).
6. If (2.24) satisfies the instability conditions (2.19) when δ = δu then δu can be computed
by solving Pa(δu) = 0.
7. If the instability conditions (2.19) are not met, the stability margin is not calculated.
If the conditions are met, the stability margin is computed utilizing (2.23).
8. Obtain a new set of measurements at time ti+1 = ti + ∆t, and repeat the procedure
from step 2. The parameters of the Pa −δ curve and predicted rotor angles are refined
recursively in accordance with section 2.3 using the measurements at time ti+1.
The procedure is terminated when the computed stability margin has converged to a
constant value or the return angle is reached according to (2.20).
2.2
Transient voltage sags due to rotor swings
Transient voltage sags occurring due to rotor swings can be illustrated by Fig. 2.2. Fig. 2.2a
shows the complex voltage at bus 2, prior to any transient disturbance, and as seen the
individual contribution from the generators add up to the voltage at bus 2. As seen when
comparing Fig. 2.2a and Fig. 2.2b the voltage at bus 2 decreases when the rotor angle of G4
increases, which is due to the occurrence of a transient disturbance. This phenomenon can
be illustrated by representing each generator by the second order classical transient stability
model, since the phase of the internal complex voltage can be represented directly by the
rotor angle of the generator [22, p. 828-829]. The simplification of the generator model
ensures a clear illustration of the impact the rotor angle has on the contribution from a
generator to the transient voltage sag.
The stator of each generator is represented by its Norton equivalent, consisting of a
current source ¯E

relating the complex current injections to the complex bus voltages is given by:
¯I = Y ¯V ,
(2.26)

by adding the admittance of the generators and loads to the respective entries.

As seen (2.27) reveals that the complex voltage at a bus is the sum of contributions of the
generators. The contribution of a generator is determined by its complex internal voltage
divided by its respective transient reactance, which is scaled and rotated by the respective
entry in the inverse of the admittance matrix [17, p. 4]. For higher order generator models
the phase of the internal complex voltage is not directly given by the rotor angle. However,
the connection between the rotor angle and the internal complex voltage is given through
the electrical torque, which is a function of the internal complex voltage, and the mismatch
between the electrical and mechanical torque relates to the change of the rotor angle.
In Fig. 2.2 a transient disturbance, e.g., a short-circuit occurs, which alters the admit-
tance matrix and leads to a change in the electric power injected by the generators, which
causes a relative acceleration or deceleration of the generators’ rotor. The change in the
rotor speed causes a change in the rotor angle. In this example to illustrate the concept it
is assumed that only G4 is affected by the fault, resulting in an acceleration relative to the
other generators, increasing the rotor angle of G4. As seen in Fig. 2.2b the voltage magnitude
at bus 2 is depressed due to the increase of G4’s rotor angle [17, p. 5].

2.2.1
Prediction of the minimum voltage magnitude during a
transient voltage sag
The minimum voltage magnitude of the transient voltage sag is predicted utilizing the candi-
dates determined transient stable by E-SIME. The minimum voltage magnitude is expected
to be reached when the group of CMs and NMs have reached their maximum angular sep-
aration, which occurs at the candidate’s return angle [13, 23].
In the following a single
candidate is considered, however, if multiple candidates are considered the approach can be
applied to each candidate, independently of the other candidates. The only requirement is
that the candidate has been declared first-swing transient stable by E-SIME.
Assume E-SIME declares the candidate first-swing transient stable at time ti, where the
current rotor angle of the candidate is δi. If the stable stability conditions (2.20) are met by
the candidate, the return angle has been reached by the candidate at time ti, hence δr = δi.
However, if (2.20) is not satisfied, the stability margin has converged to a positive value,
which indicates that E-SIME has predicted that the candidate will reach its return angle at
a time t > ti. Thus, (2.23) can be modified and the return angle reached by the candidate
can be computed by solving for δr in the following:
0 = −
Z δr
δi
Pa(δ)dδ −1
2Mω2
i ,
(2.28)
where δu has been replaced with δr, and when the R.H.S. is equal to zero it implies Aacc =
Adec and the return angle has been reached. Pa(δ) is approximated by (2.24). δi and ωi is
the rotor angle and rotor speed of the candidate at time ti, respectively. Note, Adec, i.e.,
the integral of Pa(δ) from δi to δr represents the deceleration area required for the machine
to dissipate its current kinetic energy at time ti.
The difference between the current rotor angle of the candidate and its return angle is
given by the following:
∆δr = δr −δi,
(2.29)
where ∆δr corresponds to the increase in the candidate’s rotor angle before reaching its
return angle. δi is the rotor angle of the candidate at time ti and δr its return angle.
==PAGE:32==
2.2 Transient voltage sags due to rotor swings
18
The maximum angular separation between the CMs and NMs occurs when the CMs
reach their return angle, at which point the candidate has reached its return angle. The
phase of the internal complex voltage of each CM at time ti is assumed to change with
(2.29), which enables the return angle of each CM to be computed. This assumption is used
by Weckesser et al. in [17] and considered acceptable because the change in the candidate’s
rotor angle from δi to δr occurs over a short time interval. Hence, the return angle of the
CMs is obtained by rotating the complex internal voltage of each CM by (2.29), where the
complex internal voltages are obtained from the TDS at time ti. Thus, the maximum angular
separation between the CMs and NMs has been obtained and the complex bus voltages can
be computed by solving (2.26) for ¯V , and
 ¯V
 gives the minimum bus voltage magnitudes.
2.2.2
Load voltage sensitivities
In order to identify the contribution of individual generators to a transient voltage sag, the
load voltage sensitivity matrix has to be established. This matrix informs how sensitive the
change in voltage magnitude is at each load bus per 1 rad change in the rotor angle of a
single generator. The sensitivity matrix consists of m vectors, where m corresponds to the
number of generators. Each vector has a length of n −m, corresponding to the number of
load buses where n is the total number of buses. Thus, for each load bus the sensitivity in
its voltage magnitude change for a change in the i-th generator’s rotor angle is computed
as:
(sV,Gi)ℓ= | ¯V †

obtained by solving (2.26) for ¯V † after the i-th generator’s internal complex voltage has
been shifted by ϵ. The resulting sensitivity vector with length |sV,Gi| = n −m has the unit
pu/rad or V/rad. This vector can be determined for each generator in the system and the
aggregation of these vectors form the sensitivity matrix SV,G with dimensions ((n −m) × m),
i.e., each column corresponds to a generator and each row to a load bus. Hence, SV,G is
given by the following:

p. 6].

2.3
Recursive least square estimator
The predicted rotor angles and accelerating power of each OMIB candidate is based on a
RLS estimator with exponential forgetting factor using a linear regression model as given
in (2.36) [24]. Thus, the approximations are refined recursively each time step of the TDS
using new measurements. In the following the implementation of the RLS is illustrated for
the evolution of the rotor angle (2.25), however, the approach for refining the OMIB’s Pa −δ
curve (2.24) recursively is similar.
δi = xT

(2.36)
where xi is the input vector [t2

matrix determined at time ti, and has the shape given by (2.37).

Note, that the covariance matrix only has to be inverted once in the beginning when it
has been computed using (2.38). Further, the term in the parentheses in (2.40) is a scalar
and hence no matrices are inverted. The inversion process is a costly operation and will
consequently increase computation time for larger systems, and should therefore always be
avoided if possible.
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 3
Design and implementation
In this chapter the design and implementation of the method used to perform the transient
stability assessment (TSA) and transient voltage sag assessment (TVSA) of a multimachine
system is presented. Further, the TDS tool developed is presented. Lastly, the coupling
between the method and the WARTApp platform allowing for a real-time assessment of a
multimachine system is presented, together with how the results from the TSA and TVSA
are visualized in real-time.
3.1
Overview of the method used to perform a TSA and
TVSA of a multimachine system
The implementation of the method used to perform the TSA and TVSA of a multimachine
system by screening through a list of plausible contingencies is presented in Fig. 3.1. The
method relies on a TDS tool, and since the method was implemented in Python, a TDS
tool was developed and implemented in Python, in order to ease the overall implementation.
Further, in the following a contingency is referred to as a fault scenario. The implementation
of the method in Python is included in appendix F.
The TDS tool is initialized with a set of measurements collected in steady-state or quasi
steady-state. These measurements can either be obtained from a power flow using the data
format described in section 3.2.1 or from a PMU snapshot obtained from WARTApp in
real-time. The same set of measurements are used to initialize the TDS tool for each fault
scenario considered. When a power flow is used the method will display the TSA and TVSA
results obtained from each fault scenario based on the power flow and then stop the method.
However, when the method is coupled with WARTApp it will continue the assessment of
the power system by receiving a new PMU snapshot in real-time each time the method has
screened through the list of fault scenarios. The method automatically identifies potentially
critical contingencies and locations in the power system, and visualizes the information from
the TSA and TVSA to the grid operator in a concise and easy to understand way. This
improves system awareness and enables the operator to perform preventive control actions,
if required.
The type of contingency causing transient voltage sags due to large rotor swings is
a transient disturbance, such as a transmission line fault. Whereas, events, such as a load
demand change may also cause voltage sags, however, these voltage sags are primarily caused
by the increase in current flow in the transmission lines, resulting in an increased voltage
drop across the lines. Further, the type of transmission line fault considered in this thesis is
three-phase transmission line faults considered to occur close to a bus. The fault is cleared
after a chosen time duration by placing the line out of service.

Figure 3.1: Overview of the implemented method which screens through a list of plausible contin-
gencies, each referred to as a fault scenario. For each fault scenario a TSA and TVSA is performed
if the system is first-swing transient stable.
In the following section 3.2 describes how the TDS tool has been developed and imple-
mented in Python. This tool has undergone an extensive validation process with results
obtained from Siemens PSS/E. Section 3.3 describes how E-SIME presented in section 2.1.5
has been implemented to enable the first-swing TSA. Further, section 3.4 describes how the
TVSA has been implemented. Section 3.5 goes into detail of how the real-time coupling
between the method and WARTApp has been achieved. Finally, section 3.6 describes how
the results from the method are presented to the grid operator in real-time.
3.2
Time-domain simulation tool
The power system is represented by a system of differential-algebraic equations (DAE).
˙x = F(x, y, p)
(3.1)
0 = G(x, y, p),
(3.2)
where x are the state variables, y the algebraic variables and p being parameters for the
models.
The set of differential equations F consists of the dynamical equations of the
generators, exciters, power system stabilizers and governors. Whereas, the set of algebraic
equations G consists of the network equations expressed as ¯I = Y ¯V and the stator current
==PAGE:37==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
23
equations of the generators. The network equations are used to solve for the complex bus
voltages ¯V and the stator current equations are used to calculate the d and q-component of
the stator current together with the electrical torque of the generators.
DAE systems can be solved simultaneously or partitioned. This program is based on a
partitioned solution approach, where the differential equations of the power system stabiliz-
ers, exciters, governors and generators are solved sequentially in that order, followed by the
algebraic equations. In other words, F is first solved for x and then G for y. However, prior
to solving the DAE system it must be initialized, meaning prior to performing the dynamical
simulation x and y must be known, otherwise the DAE system cannot be solved.
The program flow is presented in Fig. 3.2 and the following gives a rundown of the TDS
tool, whereas the following sections describe the various elements of the tool in greater detail.
The power flow solution is obtained using a power flow solver based on the Newton-Raphson
method developed in Python. Prior to performing the power flow, the admittance matrix Y
used to compute the power flow is constructed. The power flow computes the initial complex
bus voltages and power injections into each bus. If the power flow converges a check is made
to ensure the reactive power output of each generator is within its lower and upper limit.
The algorithm which verifies this is presented in Fig. 3.3. Thus, once it is ensured that the
obtained power flow is within physical limits the admittance of each load is calculated, and
together with the subtransient admittance, 1/jXd
′′ of each generator they are added to the
respective diagonal elements of the admittance matrix. Hence, an augmented admittance
matrix is obtained, which will be used to calculate the complex bus voltages at each time step
during the dynamical simulation. Prior to performing the dynamical simulation the initial
condition of the generators, exciters, power system stabilizers and governors is calculated,
hence x0 and y0 are obtained. The main loop of the dynamical simulation is started at t = 0
s. Next, the set of differential equations F are solved for xt via integration, and the set of
algebraic equations G solved for yt. Thereafter, a check is made if an event has occurred, and
if so the admittance matrix is refactorized accordingly. Each time the admittance matrix
is refactorized it is considered an event, this also applies for when the changes made to the
admittance matrix are reset, e.g., clearing a fault.
Once an event has occurred and the
admittance matrix has been refactorized, G is used to compute yt+, which gives the value of
the algebraic variables immediately after an event has occurred. Hence, if an event occurs
at time t the variables right before the event at t−and the ones immediately after the event
occurred at t+ are saved at this time t. The variables of interest are saved at each time step
and visualized after the simulation finishes. The desired simulation time tmax and when the
events occur and stop are set before starting the simulation. In addition, the implementation
of the TDS tool in Python is included in appendix F.1.
3.2.1
Data format of the TDS tool
The data format from Siemens PSS/E is used when importing input data to the TDS tool.
The input data consists of a raw and dyr-file. The raw-file contains steady-state information
required for the power flow computation, such as complex power injected into each bus by
generators, loads and shunts. Further, it contains all the branches and transformers in the
system. The dyr-file contains all the necessary dynamical data for the models. The raw
and dyr-file for the system used in this thesis, namely the Nordic32 system is provided in
appendix D and E, respectively.
==PAGE:38==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
24
The Python implementation of how the raw and dyr-file is loaded into the TDS tool is
included in appendix F.1.2 and F.1.3, respectively.

3.2.2
Power flow solver and reactive power output limit of
generators
The power flow is computed by solving a set of non-linear algebraic equations describing the
active and reactive power injected into each bus with a total of N buses. In the following
the power flow equations are presented and it is assumed everything is in pu except phases,
==PAGE:39==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
25
which are in rad. The implementation of the power flow solver and the reactive power output
limitation algorithm in Python is included in appendix F.1.4.
The non-linear algebraic equations for the k-th bus are given as:
Pk = Vk
N
X
n=1
YknVn cos(δk −δn −Θkn)
(3.3)
Qk = Vk
N
X
n=1
YknVn sin(δk −δn −Θkn),
(3.4)
where Pk and Qk is the active and reactive power injected into the k-th bus, respectively. Ykn
and Θkn is the magnitude and phase of the admittance between bus k and n, respectively.
Vk and δk is the magnitude and phase of the complex voltage at the k-th bus. Whereas, Vn
and δn is the magnitude and phase of the complex voltage at the n-th bus.
Buses which contain a generator are referred to as PV buses, since the active power
output of the generator and bus voltage magnitude is known. For such a bus the reactive
power output and the phase of the bus voltage is unknown. Whereas, buses which contain
a load are referred to as PQ buses, since the active and reactive power demand is known.
However, for such a bus the magnitude and phase of the complex bus voltage is unknown.
The third bus type is referred to as the slack bus or reference bus and the voltage magnitude
and phase of this bus is known, and is typically selected to be V = 1 pu and δ = 0 rad.
The active and reactive power generation are both unknowns and can be obtained after
the power flow has converged from (3.3) and (3.4).
The reference bus accounts for any
differences in the total system load demand and transmission line losses to ensure the power
flow is balanced, i.e., in steady-state. This bus is typically selected to be the generator with
the largest generation capacity to ensure active and reactive power generation obtained are
within physical limits.
Due to (3.3) and (3.4) being non-linear the unknowns cannot be solved directly, hence
an iterative approach must be applied. For this purpose the Newton-Raphson method is
used, which is in accordance with [25, p. 353-356]. For the PV buses (3.3) is used to solve
for δk iteratively, whereas Qk can be obtained directly by solving (3.4) once δk is known.
For PQ buses (3.3) and (3.4) are both used to solve for δk and Vk iteratively.
The obtained reactive power injected into a PV bus may result in an unrealistic reactive
power output for the generator connected to the bus. This has to be accounted for to ensure
a realistic power flow solution has been obtained. Consider the reactive power injected into
a bus by a generator:
QG = Qinj −Qload,
(3.5)
where QG and Qload is the reactive power injected into the bus by the generator and load,
respectively. Qinj = QG + Qload is the total amount of reactive power injected into the bus,
and it is assumed that the generator convention is used.
During the power flow Qinj can take on any value at the PV bus in order for the power
flow to converge.
However, this may result in unrealistic results for QG, therefore, it is
necessary to verify that the reactive power injected into the bus by the generator is within
its upper and lower limit:
Qmin ≤QG ≤Qmax.
(3.6)
In case multiple generators are connected to the same bus, QG represents the total
reactive power injected into the bus by the generators. Hence, for such a case the limits
==PAGE:40==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
26
Qmin and Qmax represents the aggregated minimum and maximum amount of reactive power
these generators can inject into the bus, respectively.
The power flow solver is extended in order to account for the physical limitations of
reactive power output. The PV-PQ switching algorithm presented in [26] is used and Fig. 3.3
illustrates the program flow of the algorithm. This algorithm is applied after the power flow
has converged and if the limits are exceeded the PV bus is switched to a PQ bus, and the
reactive power injected into the bus is set equal to the reactive power output limit of the
generator. The power flow is computed again using the solution from the converged power
flow as the initial guess, except for the change in the reactive power output of the generator.
Thus, once a power flow solution is obtained where none of the generators exceed their
reactive power output limit, it will be the solution considered by the TDS tool.
Compute power flow
Collect following from i-th bus:

QG,i = Qinj,i −Qload,i
PV bus?
Qmin,i < QG,i < Qmax,i
Change to PQ
QG,i = Qlimit
QG,i set to Qmax,i
last check?
Vi ≤Vi,set
Vi ≥Vi,set
Qmin,i < QG,i < Qmax,i
QG,i = Qlimit
Change to PV
Vi = Vi,set
QG,i = Qmin,i
QG,i = Qmax,i

Figure 3.3: Flow chart of the reactive power output limitation algorithm applied after a power
flow solution is obtained [26]. Vi is the voltage magnitude at the i-th bus. QG,i and Qload,i is the
aggregated reactive power injected into the i-th bus by the attached generators and loads, respectively.
Qmax,i and Qmin,i is the aggregated maximum and minimum reactive power injected into the i-th
bus by the attached generators. Qlimit represents the reactive power output limit QG,i is set equal
to. The algorithm is applied until a power flow solution which satisfies the reactive power output
limitation of the generators is obtained.

3.2.3
Solving the differential equations
Two explicit ordinary differential equation (ODE) solvers are available in the TDS tool. The
first solver is the Modified Euler method [27, p. 345-347], which is a second-order numerical
ODE solver. PSS/E also utilizes a second-order Euler integration method, and has deemed
a second-order integration method suﬀicient since numerical instability problems normally
are avoided.
Further, accuracy will be adequate if the integration time step is smaller
than about 1/5 to 1/4 of the smallest time constant [28, p. 13-10]. The other integration
method supported is the fourth-order Runge-Kutta ODE solver [29, p.
442-443].
The
implementation of the second-order Modified Euler method and fourth-order Runge-Kutta
in Python is included in appendix F.1.23 and F.1.24, respectively. The following presents
the theoretical background for these two ODE solvers.
Modified Euler
The modified Euler method is a N-stage method, given in (3.7), which is used to approximate
xn+1 from the known state xn.

n+1 ≈xn+1, which according to [30, p.

3.2.4
Solving the network equations
In order to obtain the complex voltage at each bus during the dynamical simulation, the
network equations must be solved for the complex bus voltages. The complex bus voltages
are computed during each time step or if an event has occurred. The implementation in
==PAGE:43==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
29
Python of how the network equations are solved is included in appendix F.1.9, and the
following describes the implementation.
Firstly, the admittance matrix Y is constructed, which is used for the power flow com-
putation using the following algorithm [25, p. 54]:
Yij =






(3.12)
where i represent the ’from bus’ index and j represent the ’to bus’ index in the admittance
matrix. The admittance yik is non-zero if there exists a direct connection between bus i and
k, and yi is the summation of all shunts connected to bus i. Therefore, when j = i then
all k = 1, 2, . . . , N, k ̸= i admittances connected to bus i are aggregated and when j ̸= i
then all b = 1, 2, . . . , B admittances connected between bus i and j are aggregated with a
negative sign.
The following is included in the admittance matrix when performing the power flow:
• Transmission lines, which are based on the π-equivalent.
• Transformers leakage reactance on system MVA base, which is based on a π-equivalent
seen in Fig. 3.4 to account for off-nominal turns ratio settings.
• Capacitive shunts on system MVA base.
• Inductive shunts on system MVA base.
The shunts are provided in MVAR in the raw-file using the generator convention. They
are converted to an impedance prior to performing the power flow. Thus, for PQ-buses it
is assumed the bus voltage is 1 pu. However, for PV-buses and the slack bus the known
steady-state voltage magnitude is used.
The transformers are modeled using a π-equivalent, which will account for any off-
nominal turns ratio setting, since this has an impact on the transformer’s leakage reactance.
If the transformer is at nominal turns ratio, Y2 = Y3 = 0 and Y1 = ¯Ye, with ¯Ye being the
leakage admittance of the transformer.
−
+
¯Vi
Y2
Y1
Y3

Figure 3.4: π-equivalent of the transformer, where Y1 = c ¯Ye, Y2 = c(c −1) ¯Ye, Y3 = (1 −c) ¯Ye with
¯Ye = 1/ ¯Ze being the leakage admittance and c = 1/n being the inverse of the turns ratio n in pu.
¯Vi represents the complex voltage at the ’from bus’ side and ¯Vj at the ’to bus’ side.
==PAGE:44==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
30
Prior to performing the dynamical simulation the following is added to the diagonal
elements of the admittance matrix, hence the augmented admittance matrix is obtained:
• Loads are represented by a constant admittance.
• Sub-transient saliency is neglected, i.e., X
′′
d = X
′′
q . X
′′
d is converted from generator
MVA base to system MVA base.
The armature resistance Ra has been neglected, hence only the sub-transient reactance
is added to the admittance matrix.
The complex bus voltages are obtained dynamically by solving the network equations
for the complex bus voltages ¯V , as given in the following:
¯I = Y ¯V .
(3.13)
The complex bus voltages can be solved for directly, since the network equations form
a system of linear equations. However, in order to solve for ¯V , the augmented admittance
matrix Y has to be inverted, which by use of the Gauss-Jordan inversion requires O(n3)
arithmetic operations for n equations with n unknowns. For large systems this approach is
ineﬀicient and not suitable for real-time assessment of a power system. Therefore, in order
to solve the network equations eﬀiciently the computational cost can be heavily reduced by
utilizing LU-factorization where Y is LU-factorized [31, p. 152]. The LU-factorization only
has to be performed once, and only repeated when an event occurs, due to the refactorization
of Y .
The Python implementation of the admittance matrix used for the power flow and the
augmented admittance matrix used for the dynamical simulation is included in appendix
F.1.2 and F.1.8, respectively.
3.2.5
Types of events available
The TDS tool supports four types of events:
• Three-phase line tripping.
• Three-phase line fault, which is located close to a bus.
• Generator tripping.
• Load event provided in MW.
The event is initiated and cleared at the time of choice.
A single event or multiple
events can be initiated during the dynamical simulation. Multiple events can be triggered
simultaneously or at different times. Each event can be cleared except for the generator
tripping.
When a generator is tripped it cannot be placed back into service during the
simulation, since this would require the generator to be initialized again, which requires the
system to be in a steady-state. Three-phase line faults are cleared by taking the line affected
out of service. Further, if a line is tripped and taken out of service, it can be placed back
into service if desired. Finally, a load demand change can be cleared, which reverts the load
demand back to its initial load demand during steady-state. The load demand change is
converted to an admittance and added to the respective entry in the augmented admittance
matrix. The admittance corresponding to the load demand change is computed based on
the steady-state complex bus voltage, in order to ensure the correct load demand change is
achieved when the system is in steady-state.
==PAGE:45==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
31
3.2.6
Synchronously rotating reference frame for the synchronous
generators
According to [32, p. 153] and [33, p. 101-102] the d-q components obtained for each generator
using Park’s transformation are not unique, which means each generator has individual d-
q components. This is due to each generator’s individual angle Θshaft is used during the
transformation, resulting in an individual reference frame for each generator, where Θshaft =
ωshaftt is the angle of the q-axis with respect to the network’s phase a-axis. This is not an
issue when considering each generator individually, but becomes an issue when considering
the entire power system, e.g., when solving the network equations with d-q components in
different reference frames. Therefore, in order to solve this all the individual d-q components
need a common reference frame, which is obtained using a unique transformation with
reference to the synchronously rotating reference frame, based on the synchronous speed
ωs.
This transformation is a Park transformation using the synchronous angle obtained
from the synchronous speed and its components are referred to as D-Q components. The
conversion from d-q to D-Q components is performed by first converting the d-q components
into the three-phase abc reference frame, and then into D-Q components, as presented in
the following for the i-th generator:

3.2.7
Dynamical representation of the models
IEEE generator model 2.1 and 2.2
The following two generator models included in the TDS tool, salient pole synchronous
generator (GENSAL) and round rotor synchronous generator (GENROU) are based on the
general IEEE generator model 2.1 and 2.2, respectively [35, p. 6]. According to [36, p. 44]
model 2.1 includes a single damper circuit on both the q-axis and d-axis, together with a
field circuit on the d-axis. Whereas, model 2.2 includes two damper circuits on the q-axis
and a single damper circuit together with a field circuit on the d-axis. The exact dynamical
representation of model 2.2 from where model 2.1 also originates is presented in [33, p. 34,
38], which includes the consideration of additive saturation. According to [33] model 2.2
is described by nine first-order differential equations and three algebraic equations. The
first three differential equations represent the stator voltage equations, which are neglected
==PAGE:47==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
33
by PowerWorld [34, p.
3-5] and PSS/E [28, p.
63-64].
According to [22, p.
170] it is
deemed acceptable to neglect the differential term in the stator voltage equations, since they
contain high frequency transients, which are diﬀicult to properly simulate, which makes
them diﬀicult to analyze and interpret from a system stability point of view. Furthermore,
when considering high frequency transients, small time steps are required for the numerical
integration and this will lead to increased computational costs. According to [34, p. 3-5]
and [28, p. 63-64] GENROU is modeled with a total of six first-order differential equations.
Whereas, GENSAL is modeled with a total of five first-order differential equations. For both
generator models, subtransient saliency is neglected, i.e., X
′′
d = X
′′
q .
The following presents GENROU and GENSAL, and the implementation in Python of
the dynamical model for GENROU and GENSAL is included in appendix F.1.10 and F.1.12,
respectively. Whereas, the implementation in Python of how the algebraic equations are
solved is included in appendix F.1.14.
GENROU
GENROU is a sixth-order generator model, which is used to model round rotor synchronous
generators and is represented by the block diagram in Fig. 3.5 when saturation is considered.

TGOV1

During steady-state ∆ω = 0, which implies the generator’s mechanical power is given
by:
Pm = Pref,
(3.52)
where Pref = Pm0 is the generator’s mechanical power in steady-state.
Whereas, when a change in the rotor speed occurs due to a mismatch between Pe and
Pm, then the governor’s control actions will cause a change in Pm, and once ∆ω settles at a
value, then Pm will stop changing, which implies that Pe and Pm have become equal.

describing the dynamical behavior of TGOV1. These two differential equations are obtained
directly from Fig. 3.10 and are presented in the following:

HYGOV
The block diagram of the hydro-turbine governor (HYGOV) supported by PSS/E [28, p.
254] is illustrated in Fig. 3.12 and Table 3.13 contains an overview of the different variables.
==PAGE:56==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
42
The implementation of the dynamical model for HYGOV in Python is included in appendix
F.1.17.
HYGOV models both the governor, located in the upper part of Fig. 3.12 and the hydro-
turbine of the generator, located in the lower part. The difference between the desired gate
opening and the actual gate opening is the time it takes for the servomotor controlling the
gate opening to respond, since the gate cannot change its position instantaneously. The
desired gate opening is limited by a non-windup limit, ensuring the gate cannot open by
more than its limits Gmax and Gmin and prevents ˙c from increasing once c is fixed at its
limits. Further, the desired gate opening velocity ˙c is also limited by ±V ELM, which applies
to both the gate opening and closing, where ˙c > 0 indicates the gate is opening and ˙c < 0
indicates the gate is closing.
The speed droop logic used by the governor is represented by a linear relationship between
the rotor speed and gate position with the permanent droop being the slope [22, p. 590].
This implies that depending on the rotor speed the gate position will change accordingly,
and the amount it changes depends on the permanent droop. Thus, as seen in Fig. 3.12 the
current gate position ci is fed back and used to calculate the rotor speed ωc,i = Rci which
matches this gate position. However, since the actual rotor speed may have changed further
since the gate position was set, this change ∆ω is added to ωc,i. Hence, a new rotor speed
is obtained ωc,i+1 = ωc,i + ∆ω, which will result in a changed gate position ci+1 matching
ωc,i+1. However, in order to obtain the actual required change in the gate position, the new
rotor speed ωc,i+1 has to be provided with respect to the load reference set-point nref. The
result is the so called error term d = nref−ωc,i+1, which is used to compute the change in the
gate position. The variable d represents the deviation in the rotor speed from synchronous
speed, however, with an opposite sign compared to the actual speed deviation, since the
controller is counteracting the rotor speed change.

SEXS
The exciter implemented in the TDS tool is IEEE’s simplified excitation system (SEXS),
supported by PSS/E [28, p. 609], which is illustrated in Fig. 3.14. This type of voltage
regulator is also referred to an automatic voltage regulator (AVR). Table 3.15 contains an
overview of the different variables. The implementation of the dynamical model for SEXS
in Python is included in appendix F.1.19.
The objective of the AVR is to maintain a constant bus voltage magnitude at the gen-
erator’s terminals. This is achieved by controlling the field voltage of the generator, hence
the output of the AVR is the set-point of the generator’s field voltage.
In case the bus
voltage magnitude decreases below the rated voltage the AVR will cause the field voltage of
the generator to increase, resulting in an increased induced voltage magnitude in the stator
winding, causing the bus voltage magnitude at the terminals to increase. Whereas, if the bus
voltage magnitude increases beyond the rated voltage the AVR will cause the field voltage
to decrease in order to lower the bus voltage magnitude at the terminals.
The bus voltage magnitude is an input to the AVR, provided as a compensated bus
voltage magnitude Vc which is given by the following:
Vc =
 ¯Vt + (Rc + jXc) ¯It
 ,
(3.59)
where ¯Vt is the complex bus voltage and (Rc + jXc) ¯It is the voltage drop across the load
compensator, with ¯It being the complex stator current.
The load compensator, which consists of the impedance (Rc + jXc) is used to control
the magnitude of the compensated voltage Vc which is the input to the AVR. With Rc and
Xc positive the voltage drop across the compensator is added to the terminal voltage, which
means a voltage within the generator is regulated. Thus, the regulation of Vc due to the
compensator provides a voltage droop, where (Rc + jXc) is the droop impedance. Hence, by
varying the droop impedance, different voltage droop characteristics can be obtained.
The usage of a voltage droop to control Vc is important to ensure proper sharing of
reactive power between generators sharing the same bus. Without the load compensator,
then Vc =
 ¯Vt
 and one of the generators would be controlling the terminal voltage slightly
higher than the others. This would tend to cause an increase in this generator’s reactive
power output, while the other generators would become underexcited and absorb reactive
power to the extent of their underexcited limits [22, p. 336]. Therefore, a load compensator
should be attached to each generator with an AVR when they are sharing the same bus
in order to prevent this from occurring, and to enable the control of Vc to ensure that
==PAGE:59==
3.2 Time-domain simulation tool
45
the field voltage is changed by the appropriate amount for each generator. However, load
compensators have not been implemented in the TDS tool, hence Vc =
 ¯Vt
.
Vc

 ¯Vt + (Rc + jXc) ¯It
.
VOTHSG is the output of the power system stabilizer (PSS), which is an input to the AVR.
The PSS available in the TDS tool is introduced in section 3.2.7. Further, no under and
over excitation limits are considered except for the non-windup limits on the time-constant
block which limits c and its derivative ˙c, hence VUEL = VOEL = 0.

derivative for z, an auxiliary variable Φ is used, which does not depend on the derivative
of the input to the lead-lag block.

not known, hence without the usage of an auxiliary variable the implementation would be

complicated. Therefore, this results in an algebraic equation for z, which is dependent on

The output of SEXS is the field voltage set-point for the generator, which is given by
the following:
Efd = c.

STAB2A
The block diagram of the power-sensitive stabilizing unit (STAB2A) supported by PSS/E
[28] is illustrated in Fig. 3.16 and Table 3.17 contains an overview of the different variables.
The implementation of the dynamical model for STAB2A in Python is included in appendix
F.1.21.
The objective of the PSS is to add damping to the generator rotor oscillations, which is
achieved by contributing with an auxiliary stabilizing signal to the AVR when controlling
the field voltage.
Physically the PSS must produce a component of electrical torque in
phase with the rotor speed deviations in order to provide damping of the generator rotor
oscillations [22, p.
766].
When the external system reactance and generator output is
high the AVR produces a positive synchronizing torque and a negative damping torque.
The positive synchronizing torque improves first-swing transient stability, however, due to
the negative damping torque it may result in any subsequent oscillations being negatively
damped, depending on the system, and this can lead to small-signal instability [22, p. 766].
The PSS complements the AVR by increasing the damping torque in order to dampen
any subsequent rotor oscillations and prevent small-signal instability. The PSS accomplishes
this without compromising the synchronizing torque acting on the rotor [22, p. 769-770].
