Loads
The loads are modeled as constant admittances during the dynamical simulation. Whereas,
during the power flow each load is modeled by their P0, Q0 consumption. Therefore, when
converting the load to an admittance, the steady-state voltage magnitude V0 at the load bus
obtained from the power flow is used as given by the following:
Yload = P0 −jQ0

3.2.8
Initialization of the models during steady-state
The following describes how GENROU, GENSAL, TGOV1, HYGOV, SEXS and STAB2A
is initialized. The initialization of the controllers is obtained directly from the differential
equations, however, the initialization for GENROU and GENSAL is in accordance with
[33, p. 40-42] and [38]. The implementation in Python of the initialization for GENROU,
GENSAL, TGOV1, HYGOV, SEXS and STAB2A is presented in appendix F.1.11, F.1.13,
F.1.16, F.1.18, F.1.20 and F.1.22, respectively.

3.3
Implementation of E-SIME
E-SIME has been implemented in accordance with section 2.1.5, and Fig. 3.18 illustrates the
program flow of the implementation. The implementation in Python of E-SIME is included
in appendix F.2.3.
After fault clearance the power system enters its post-fault configuration from where the
first three successive measurements at times t1 = ti −2∆t, t2 = ti −∆t, t3 = ti of each
generator’s rotor angle, rotor speed, mechanical and electrical power is collected. The rotor
angle evolution of each generator is estimated in accordance with the RLS estimator in sec-
tion 2.3 using (2.25). The rotor angle evolution of each generator is predicted ahead of time,
==PAGE:68==
3.3 Implementation of E-SIME
54
e.g., 100 ms, which enables the identification of possible OMIB candidates in accordance
with section 2.1.4 and the implementation is presented in Fig. 3.19. The parameters of the
identified OMIB candidates are computed at times t1, t2, t3, which enables the Pa −δ curve
in (2.24) for each candidate to be estimated in accordance with the RLS estimator in section
2.3, and the implementation is presented in Fig. 3.20.
If the candidate satisfies condition (2.20), the candidate has reached its return angle and
is declared transient stable, without the need for computing a stability margin. However,
if the candidate has not reached its return angle, and condition (2.19) is satisfied for the
estimated Pa −δ curve a stability margin in accordance with (2.23) is computed.
The
initial stability margin is computed from the measurements at time t3, however, subsequent
measurements are required in order to ensure the stability margin has converged. In order
to determine if the stability margin has converged the absolute relative error between the
current and previously computed stability margin is utilized as given by the following:
Γ =

ηj −ηj−1
ηj
 ,
(3.117)
where ηj is the stability margin computed based on the current set of measurements and
ηj−1 is the previously computed stability margin. Both computed in accordance with (2.23).
In this thesis the stability margin has converged to a steady value if the following condi-
tion is satisfied:
Γ < 20 %.
(3.118)
There is a clear trade-off between the time E-SIME uses to perform the first-swing TSA
of the system and the accuracy of the stability margin.
With (3.118) more strict, e.g.,
Γ < 1 % the accuracy of the TSA is increased, however, it results in an increased run time
of E-SIME. Increasing the run time of E-SIME may result in the actual minimum of the
transient voltage sag occurring during the TDS, which prevents the method from properly
predicting the minimum. Originally, Γ < 10 % was used, however, it was observed that the
run time of E-SIME was too long, causing it to exceed the actual minimum of the transient
voltage sag. However, by utilizing Γ < 20 % instead of Γ < 10 % the run time of E-SIME
was reduced with around 200 ms for several cases, which resulted in E-SIME concluding its
TSA prior to the TDS reaching the actual minimum of the transient voltage sag.
The subsequent measurements are obtained at each time step during the TDS, which
are used to refine the approximation of each generator’s rotor angle evolution using the
RLS estimator, which enables the identification of new OMIB candidates.
Further, the
parameters of each existing candidate are updated, used to refine its Pa −δ curve utilizing
the RLS estimator.
The implementation of how a candidate is refined is illustrated in
Fig. 3.21. If a candidate is identified at time tTDS > t3 the previous measurements from time
[t3+∆t, tTDS−∆t] are used to refine the new candidate. When the new candidate reaches
the current simulation time tTDS it is refined alongside the existing candidates using the
measurements collected at this time. The previous measurements have been stored and can
be applied directly, which results in a reduction of the method’s run time, since the stability
margin of the new candidate may converge based solely on the previous measurements.
Once the candidates have been refined at the current simulation time tTDS a stability check
is performed. If any of the candidates has a stability margin below or equal to zero, which is
in accordance with (3.118) the E-SIME algorithm is stopped and the multimachine system
declared transient unstable. Whereas, if all the current candidates have a stability margin
==PAGE:69==
3.3 Implementation of E-SIME
55
above zero, which is in accordance with (3.118) the E-SIME algorithm is stopped and the
multimachine system declared transient stable.

length |CM| + |NM| = m, and they contain the indices of the critical and non-critical machines,

the field voltage of the generator.

==PAGE:76==
3.5 Real-time implementation with WARTApp
62
3.5
Real-time implementation with WARTApp
The coupling between the method and WARTApp is presented in Fig. 3.25, which illustrates
that a PMU snapshot collected in real-time is used to perform the TSA and TVSA for
each fault scenario. The PMU snapshot contains the current operating point of the power
system, which the method utilizes to predict the transient stability and possible occurrence
of a transient voltage sag in case one of the fault scenarios were to occur in the future. The
PMU snapshot is used to initialize the models used in the TDS tool.
Hence, the PMU
snapshot must be collected in either a steady-state or quasi steady-state, since the models
are initialized in accordance with section 3.2.8 which assumes the power system is in steady-
state. Further, WARTApp contains a model database of the equipment used in the power
system, which is provided to the TDS tool. When the TSA and TVSA has been performed
for each fault scenario, a new PMU snapshot is collected in real-time and the assessment
is performed again. The results obtained from the TSA and TVSA for each fault scenario
is displayed in a table, which is updated in real-time once the TSA and TVSA has been
performed on a new PMU snapshot.
The PMU snapshot provided by WARTApp simulates the collection of PMU measure-
ments obtained from the power system at a given time. PMUs are able to transmit complex
voltage and current measurements synchronized in time utilizing GPS, and several PMU
measurements can be transmitted each second [39]. Hence, PMUs provide a high quantity
and quality of information which is utilized by the stability assessment methods [11].
The preloaded simulations performed by WARTApp have been deemed suﬀicient for
testing and validating the method for assessing transient voltage sags in real-time. Hence,
the implementation of preventive control actions and visualizing their effect in real-time
using the RTDS is outside the scope of this thesis.

3.5 Real-time implementation with WARTApp
63
The PMU snapshot provided to the method consists of the following:
• Complex bus voltages.
• Complex generator currents.
• Complex branch currents.
The WARTApp platform also provides the TDS tool with the augmented admittance
matrix for the entire power system, which has been constructed based on the PMU snapshot.
The structure of the augmented admittance matrix is identical to the description provided
in section 3.2.4. In addition, WARTApp computes the total complex power injected into a
bus based on the branches connected to the bus utilizing the complex bus voltage at the bus
and the complex branch currents as shown in the following:
¯Sinj = ¯Vt

X
b=1
¯Ib
!∗
= ¯SG + ¯Sload + ¯Sshunt,
(3.119)
where ¯SG, ¯Sload, ¯Sshunt is the complex power injected into the bus by the generator, load
and shunt, respectively, using the generator convention. ¯Ib is the complex branch current of
branch b with a total of B branches connected to the bus. ′∗′ denotes the complex conjugate.
Based on the complex generator current and the complex bus voltage the complex power
injected into the bus by the generator is given by:
¯SG = ¯Vt ¯I∗
a ,
(3.120)
where ¯Ia is the complex generator current and ¯Vt the complex bus voltage. ′∗′ denotes the
complex conjugate.
Further, based on the shunt’s admittance obtained from its rated voltage and injected
complex power in steady-state, the complex power injected into the bus by the shunt is given
by the following:
¯Sshunt = ¯Vt
 yshunt ¯Vt
∗,
(3.121)
where yshunt is the admittance of the shunt obtained in steady-state and ¯Vt the complex bus
voltage. ′∗′ denotes the complex conjugate.
This allows for the computation of the complex power injected into the bus by the load,
which is provided to the TDS tool:
¯Sload = ¯Sinj −¯SG −¯Sshunt.
(3.122)
The obtained complex power injected into the bus in (3.119) includes the contribution
from the shunt, however, in the TDS the shunt is included in the admittance matrix as
described in section 3.2.4. This implies that when a power flow is performed the complex
power injected into the bus only consists of the complex power injected into the bus by the
generator and load. The complex power injected into the bus is used when initializing the
generators as described in section 3.2.8. Thus, in order to comply with this design choice,
WARTApp provides the TDS tool with the following injected complex power:
¯Sinj,TDS = ¯SG + ¯Sload.
(3.123)
The Python implementation of the screening mechanism, which screens through the list
of fault scenarios is included in appendix F.3.2. Further, the Python implementation of the
TDS tool, which is coupled with the screening mechanism and performs the TSA and TVSA
is included in appendix F.3.1. Lastly, the Python implementation of the coupling between
the method and WARTApp is included in appendix F.3.3.
==PAGE:78==
3.5 Real-time implementation with WARTApp
64
3.5.1
Determination of quasi steady-state
The occurrence of a transient disturbance causes the power system to oscillate, which causes
dynamical changes in the system. Once the disturbance has been cleared these oscillations
will be damped over time, given the system remains stable. Hence, as the oscillations are
damped the system will approach a new steady-state, and at some point the dynamical
changes in the system are so slow the system can be considered to be in a quasi steady-
state. In order to determine if the system has reached a quasi steady-state the change in the
internal voltage magnitude of each generator is utilized. In order to determine how slow the
changes should be before declaring the system in a quasi steady-state, the time constants
for the two generator models, GENROU and GENSAL are examined. The time constant is
provided in seconds, and is the time it takes for the system’s response to change with 63.2%,
and the change in the system’s response is an exponential change. For a decaying response
this percentage change is based on the initial value of the system, whereas for an increasing
response it is based on the final value reached by the system. In order to obtain a conservative
threshold for when to declare the system in a quasi steady-state, the largest time constant is
considered, since it results in the slowest change of the response. The d-axis transient time
constant for the two generator models GENROU and GENSAL is the largest time constant,
which ranges between 5 s and 7 s in this thesis. In order to obtain the threshold, assume a
step decrease causing the system’s response to decay from an initial value of 1 pu. Further, a
linear change is assumed from the initial value to 0.37 pu, which for the two time constants
roughly corresponds to a change of 0.13 pu/s and 0.09 pu/s, respectively. Hence, based on
this the threshold is selected to be below 0.09 pu/s, and in order to increase the certainty
that the system has reached a quasi steady-state the threshold is selected to be 0.01 pu/s.
This implies that the system is declared to have reached a quasi steady-state if the absolute
value of the internal voltage magnitude change for each generator is lower than 0.01 pu/s.
In order to account for variations in the PMU snapshots, a moving average is used which
computes the average change in the internal voltage magnitude of each generator based on
11 PMU snapshots.
WARTApp has been set to receive 25 PMU snapshots each second,
which implies the distance between two PMU snapshots is 0.04 s, which corresponds to a
time interval of 0.44 s being covered when considering the 11 PMU snapshots.
In order to compute the internal complex voltage of the i-th generator its subtransient
reactance is obtained from the model database in WARTApp, and from the current PMU
snapshot the complex bus voltage and the complex generator current is obtained. Hence,
the internal voltage magnitude for the i-th generator is given by the following:
E
′′
i =
 ¯Vt + jX
′′
d,i ¯Ia,i
 ,
(3.124)
where ¯Vt is the complex bus voltage, X
′′
d,i and ¯Ia,i the subtransient reactance and complex
generator current of the i-th generator, respectively.
Thus, the internal voltage magnitude is computed in accordance with (3.124) for two
subsequent PMU snapshots at times tj and tj−1. Hence, the k-th internal voltage magnitude
change with respect to time can be computed for the i-th generator:

3.6
Visualization of the results from the TSA and TVSA
The information obtained from the TSA and TVSA is provided to the grid operator utilizing
a table structure as seen in Fig. 3.26. In order to quickly inform the grid operator of the
critical cases a color scheme is used. The usage of a table combined with a color scheme
enables the large amount of information to be provided to the grid operator in a concise and
easy to understand way. This improves system awareness and enables the grid operator to
perform preventive control actions and investigate critical cases in further detail, if required.
As previously stated the type of fault scenario considered in this thesis is three-phase
transmission line faults. Each row in Fig. 3.26 represents a fault scenario, and each column
contains the following information:
• Bus from and bus to indicates the two buses the transmission line is connected to.
• Transmission line id.
• Result from the TSA.
• Severity of the TVSA.
• ℓ-th load bus which experience a transient voltage sag.
• Total voltage magnitude change ∆Vℓat the ℓ-th load bus.
• Minimum voltage magnitude Vmin,ℓat ℓ-th load bus.
• Location of the k-th generator which results in the largest depression of the ℓ-th load
bus voltage magnitude.
• k-th generator id.
• Contribution of the k-th generator ∆V ⋆
ℓ,k to the voltage magnitude change of the ℓ-th
load bus.
==PAGE:80==
3.6 Visualization of the results from the TSA and TVSA
66
The color scheme used for the TSA:
• Green: Power system is first-swing transient stable.
• Red: Power system is first-swing transient unstable.
• Gray: E-SIME was unable to determine the system’s transient stability.
According to [14] the transient voltage magnitude is not permitted to reduce below 0.7
pu. Thus, for any voltage sags reducing below 0.7 pu the TVSA is declared critical, which
is marked by the color red. Based on this four zones, each identified by a unique color were
established for the TVSA results when assessing the ℓ= 1, . . . , L load buses:
• Green: All L load buses satisfy | ¯Vℓ| > 0.9 pu.
• Orange: All L load buses satisfy | ¯Vℓ| > 0.8 pu, with at least one load bus | ¯Vℓ| ≤0.9 pu.
• Yellow: All L load buses satisfy | ¯Vℓ| > 0.7 pu, with at least one load bus | ¯Vℓ| ≤0.8 pu.
• Red: At least one load bus | ¯Vℓ| ≤0.7 pu.
• Gray: The power system is either transient unstable or E-SIME was unable to deter-
mine the system’s transient stability.
In order to keep the table manageable, the numerical data from the TVSA is only
provided for the critical scenarios and near-critical scenarios marked by red and yellow,
respectively. Hence, if the scenario is marked by either orange or green these columns are
marked by the color gray. For the cases which are either marked by orange or green the
coloring is suﬀicient information for the grid operator, since it informs the grid operator that
the possible voltage sags occurring for these scenarios are not of concern. Furthermore, if
a transmission line has been placed out of service in the power system it will be indicated
by ”Line is offline” as seen for line 4046–4047 in Fig. 3.26, and it will not be considered by
the method as a fault scenario. Also, in case multiple transient voltage sags with the same
severity occur at different load buses for the same fault scenario, they will each be displayed
in the table and their information separated by a comma, as seen for fault scenario 4043–4047
in Fig. 3.26.
The table is updated in real-time based on the current PMU snapshot provided by
WARTApp to the method. Hence, when the TSA and TVSA has been performed for all the
fault scenarios the table is updated with the new results. More specifically, the results from
the TSA and TVSA are published to the WARTApp platform, from where they are sent to a
Python script included in appendix F.3.4. This script visualizes the results in real-time each
time a new set of results from the TSA and TVSA has been published to the WARTApp
platform.

==PAGE:82==
CHAPTER 4
Results
This chapter presents the validation of the method, which is performed based on two event
cases. Further, the type of preventive control action which can be applied to reduce the
severity of the transient voltage sag is investigated. The validation is based on the Nordic32
system, and the system topology is included in appendix A.
4.1
List of events for validation of the method
The method is validated based on two events.
During the first event the power system
remains in a steady-state during the preloaded simulation provided by WARTApp. Whereas,
for the second event a transient disturbance occurs during the preloaded simulation. The
first event enables the validation of the method’s assessment in a real-time application where
the obtained PMU snapshots are in steady-state.
Whereas, the second event validates
the method’s assessment after the occurrence of a transient disturbance in the preloaded
simulation.
In addition, each fault scenario during the screening consists of a three-phase transmis-
sion line fault lasting for 190 ms and is cleared by placing the line out of service.
The
reason for using 190 ms is due to the fact that using 200 ms causes several of the fault
scenarios to become transient unstable. However, in order to validate the method, the fault
duration should be long enough in order to provoke the occurrence of transient voltage sags.
Moreover, it was found that a transient voltage sag at load bus 2031 would be observed if
line 4022–4031 (2) was removed in the raw-file prior to generating the preloaded simulation.
Hence, for both events this line has been removed.
4.1.1
Description of event 1
During this event the power system remains in a steady-state, which implies no dynamical
changes occur during the preloaded simulation. The preloaded simulation length is 200 s.
4.1.2
Description of event 2
This event is similar to event 1, however, with the addition of a fault on line 4046–4047
occurring at t = 0 s during the preloaded simulation and being cleared after 200 ms by
removing line 4046–4047. The preloaded simulation length is 200 s.
4.1.3
Overview of the GEN id and load bus number
The following provides an overview of where each generator and load is located in the
Nordic32 system, together with the identifier used for each generator and load.

Figure 4.2: TSA and TVSA results obtained for event 1.
As seen the method has predicted that if a fault was to occur on either of the two parallel
lines 2031–2032, a transient voltage sag with a critically low voltage magnitude of 0.67 pu
would occur at load bus 2031. The method predicts that the voltage magnitude at load bus
2031 decreases with 0.16 pu, where generator 2032 contributes with a depression of 0.16 pu.
The contribution from the other generators is negligible and not visible when considering
two decimals. Furthermore, the fault scenarios where a fault occurs on either of the lines
==PAGE:85==
4.2 Validation of the method
71
connected to bus 1042, the voltage magnitude at bus 142, connected to bus 1042 via a
transformer experiences a voltage sag of 0.71 pu with the largest depression of 0.10 pu from
the generator at bus 1042. Finally, for fault scenario 4042–4043 and 4042–4044 the voltage
magnitude at bus 42 connected to bus 4042 via a transformer, experiences a transient voltage
sag with a predicted minimum of 0.80 pu, and the generator at bus 4021 contributing with
the largest depression of 0.06 pu. Whereas, for fault scenario 4043–4046, bus 46 connected to
bus 4046 via a transformer experiences a transient voltage sag of 0.8 pu where the generator
at bus 4047 contributes with the largest depression of 0.04 pu. For the other fault scenarios
there is a mix between cases which are predicted to be first-swing transient unstable, and
some with a transient voltage sag in the green and orange zone.
In order to validate the results from the method some of the fault scenarios are investi-
gated in further detail by using the TDS results obtained utilizing the same PMU snapshot
used by the method to produce the results in Fig. 4.2.
Fault scenario 2031–2032
Firstly the fault scenario with a line fault on 2031–2032 is investigated. E-SIME declares
the power system first-swing transient stable 150 ms after fault clearance, and as seen from
Fig. 4.3 the voltage at bus 2031 has started to approach its minimum. However, E-SIME
stops the TDS prior to reaching the actual minimum, which implies the method is able to
predict the minimum reached at bus 2031.

Figure 4.3: E-SIME stops the TDS 150 ms after fault clearance and declares the system transient
stable for fault scenario 2031–2032.
During these 150 ms E-SIME identifies four OMIB candidates, as seen in Fig. 4.4, which
illustrates how the stability margin converges for each candidate. For each time step the
==PAGE:86==
4.2 Validation of the method
72
stability margin of each candidate is computed if the candidate satisfies condition (2.19).
The stability margin between candidates cannot be compared directly, since the gained
kinetic energy by each candidate does not change by the same proportion for varying fault
durations.
The four candidates identified by E-SIME consist of the following number of CMs and
NMs: Candidate 0 consists of 10 CMs and 12 NMs, candidate 1 consists of 1 CM and 21
NMs, candidate 2 consists of 15 CMs and 7 NMs and candidate 3 consists of 8 CMs and 14
NMs. As seen candidate 2 was the last to be declared first-swing transient stable, whereas
candidate 1 was the fastest. The method declared candidate 1 the candidate of interest,
since it resulted in the lowest voltage magnitude observed at one of the load buses. Further,
as seen for candidate 0 and 2, condition (2.19) has first been satisfied at time 0.24 s and
0.27 s, respectively. The stability margin of candidate 1 was the fastest to converge at 0.22 s.
Moreover, the method predicted that candidate 1 would reach its return angle after its rotor
angle had increased with ∆δr = 50.2◦. Hence, candidate 1’s single CM, corresponding to
GEN-7 is shifted with this amount from the time candidate 1 was declared transient stable
which is at 0.22 s, and according to the method a minimum voltage magnitude of 0.67 pu is
reached at load bus 2031 once GEN-7 reaches its return angle.

step. Candidate 0 consists of 10 CMs and 12 NMs. Candidate 1 consists of 1 CM and 21 NMs.
Candidate 2 consists of 15 CMs and 7 NMs. Candidate 3 consists of 8 CMs and 14 NMs. The

The contribution of each generator to the voltage magnitude change experienced by
==PAGE:87==

each load bus from fault clearance until the return angle has been reached by candidate
1’s CM, i.e., GEN-7 is seen in Fig. 4.5. Some of the generators contribute positively and
some negatively, and the sum of the contributions for a given load bus results in the total
voltage magnitude change experienced by the load bus. As seen for load bus number 25
corresponding to load bus 2031 the negative contribution is much larger than the positive
contribution. The contribution from GEN-7 is heavily negative, relative to the other gener-
ators, and according to Fig. 4.5 is the primary cause for the voltage sag experienced at bus
2031 as indicated in Fig. 4.2

Figure 4.5: Contribution of each generator to the voltage magnitude change at each load bus from
fault clearance until the single CM (GEN-7) of candidate 1 reach its return angle. Positive contribu-
tion increases the voltage magnitude, and a negative contribution decreases the voltage magnitude.
The sum of the contributions for each load bus corresponds to the total voltage magnitude change
at the load bus.
The actual voltage magnitude evolution for the load buses is seen in Fig. 4.6, whereas the
rotor angle evolution of each generator is seen in Fig. 4.7. As seen load bus 2031 experiences
a transient voltage sag after the line fault has been cleared. Further, GEN-7 is the only
generator accelerating relative to the other generators, which is in accordance with candidate
1, since it contains a single CM. GEN-7 reaches its return angle at 0.6 s, implying the power
system is first-swing transient stable as predicted by the method. However, as seen from
Fig. 4.6 the actual minimum voltage magnitude occur around 0.5 s with a voltage magnitude
of 0.72 pu, which indicates the actual minimum occurs prior to GEN-7 reaching its return
angle. This may be due to the field voltage of GEN-7 being increased by its AVR from 0.45 s
to around 0.68 s as seen in Fig. 4.8. The effect of the field voltage increase is visible at bus
2032 at 0.6 s where the bus voltage magnitude starts to increase. However, as seen the voltage
change at bus 2032 is reflected at bus 2031, which experiences the same dynamical changes
in its voltage magnitude. These two buses are only separated by the voltage drop across
line 2031–2032, hence any voltage change at bus 2032 is reflected at bus 2031. Moreover,
as seen around 0.7 s in Fig. 4.8 when the rotor angle of GEN-7 decreases and approaches
the other generators the voltage magnitude at bus 2031 increases, which is reflected at bus
==PAGE:88==
4.2 Validation of the method
74
2032.
Hence, both the reduction in GEN-7’s rotor angle and the increased field voltage
contributes to a rapid increase in the bus voltage magnitude at bus 2031 and 2032 from
0.7−1.0 s. The AVR detects this increase in the voltage magnitude at bus 2032, and rapidly
decreases GEN-7’s field voltage in order to prevent over voltages.
Furthermore, once E-SIME declares the power system first-swing transient stable, the
rotor angle of GEN-7 with respect to the COI when candidate 1 was declared first-swing
transient stable was 35.1◦at time 0.22 s, and according to the method GEN-7 should reach its
return angle at δr = 35.1◦+50.2◦= 85.3◦, which is 1.7◦below the actual return angle at 87◦.
Hence, the predicted return angle is close to the actual return angle, however, the method
does not consider the effect of GEN-7’s AVR on bus 2031’s voltage magnitude during the
transient voltage sag. As seen from Fig. 4.8 the trajectory of the voltage magnitude evolution
of bus 2031 is changed as the field voltage starts to increase at 0.45 s. Therefore, based on
this observation it is likely that the predicted minimum of 0.67 pu would have been reached
at bus 2031 if GEN-7 did not contain an AVR.

the rotor angle evolutions being grouped together. Hence, an incorrect result is obtained by
E-SIME, causing it to falsely identify these fault scenarios as first-swing transient unstable.

Fault scenarios with a TVSA in the green and orange zone
The power system remains first-swing transient stable for each of these fault scenarios. Fur-
ther, the voltage magnitudes at the load buses are within the green and orange zone, as
predicted by the TVSA.
Fig. 4.16 shows the rotor angle evolution for fault scenario 1013–1014 and as seen the
power system remains first-swing transient stable.
Further, Fig. 4.17 shows the voltage
magnitude evolution, and as seen all load buses except 1011 experience a transient voltage
sag. However, none of them reduce below 0.90 pu, as predicted by the method since the
TVSA result is in the green zone.
Whereas, the voltage magnitude evolution for fault
scenario 4044–4045 is seen in Fig. 4.19, and the other fault scenarios with a TVSA in the
orange zone have a similar voltage magnitude evolution. As seen there does not occur a
voltage sag, however, the voltage is as predicted by the method below 0.90 pu, which is
due to the voltage recovering at a voltage magnitude below 0.90 pu. The OMIB candidates
identified by E-SIME reach their return angle during the E-SIME assessment, which is
prior to the voltage increasing above 0.90 pu, hence the reason the TVSA is in the orange
zone. The rotor angle evolution for fault scenario 4044–4045 is grouped together as seen
in Fig. 4.18, which makes it diﬀicult for E-SIME to identify candidates, which describe the
actual behavior.

Fault scenario 1042–1044 and 1042–1045
Fault scenario 1042–1044 and 1042–1045 result in similar rotor angle and voltage magnitude
evolutions, hence in the following it is suﬀicient to only consider fault scenario 1042–1044.
E-SIME identified three candidates at fault clearance: Candidate 0 contains 21 CMs and
1 NM, candidate 1 contains 19 CMs and 3 NMs and candidate 2 contains 10 CMs and 12 NMs.
Each candidate reached their return angle during the E-SIME assessment, hence ∆δr = 0
and the CMs are not shifted. This means that the CMs have reached their return angle at
2.2 s when E-SIME stops the TDS and declares the system transient stable. This implies
that if a transient voltage sag has occurred it occurs at 2.2 s. However, as seen in Fig. 4.20
the lowest voltage at this time is observed at load bus 142, and it has not experienced a
voltage sag at this time. According to Fig. 4.21 load bus 142 experiences a voltage sag at
a later time and reaches a minimum voltage magnitude of 0.78 pu at around 0.5 s. GEN-5
reaches its return angle at this time as seen in Fig. 4.22, however, it seems E-SIME has been
unable to determine the correct candidate, which is suspected to be due to the rotor angle
evolution of GEN-5 being grouped with the other generators, which makes it diﬀicult for
E-SIME to identify this generator as a CM. Therefore, based on the rotor angle evolution
of each generator in Fig. 4.22 none of the candidates identified by E-SIME match the actual
behavior, hence the reason for the incorrect prediction of the transient voltage sag.

Figure 4.22: Actual rotor angle evolution of the generators with respect to the COI. Obtained
from the TDS tool for fault scenario 1042–1044.
4.2.2
Event 2
The results from the TSA and TVSA are presented in Fig. 4.23, and the total run time of
the method to screen through all fault scenarios was 37.1 s. The fault during the preloaded
simulation occurs at t = 0 s and is cleared at t = 0.20 s. The power system was declared to
be in a quasi steady-state at t = 8.64 s. Hence, the system was declared to be in a quasi
steady-state 8.44 s after fault clearance, which corresponds to a total of 211 PMU snapshots.
211 PMU snapshots result in 21 windows each consisting of 11 successive PMU snapshots.
Hence, for the first window 11 PMU snapshots are collected, however, for the following only
10 are collected, since the 11th snapshot is included in the second window.
In order to validate the results from the method some of the fault scenarios are investi-
gated in further detail by using the TDS results obtained utilizing the same PMU snapshot
used by the method to produce the results in Fig. 4.23.

As seen from Fig. 4.23 which is based on the PMU snapshot at t = 8.64 s it is indicated
that line 4046–4047 is offline, since it was placed out of service when the fault was cleared.
Further, E-SIME was unable to determine transient stability for fault scenario 4043–4047.
Fault scenario 4043–4046 indicates that the voltage magnitude at load bus 46 is 0 pu, however,
fault scenario 4043–4047 does not result in a critical transient voltage sag at load bus 46.
Load bus 46 is connected to bus 4046 via a transformer and if this fault scenario occurs
==PAGE:100==
4.2 Validation of the method
86
in the future, bus 4046 will be isolated from the rest of the system, hence bus 4046 will
have a voltage of 0 pu. However, it was decided not to add an additional feature to handle
isolated buses, since the grid operator can refer to the grid topology and confirm the bus
will become isolated if this fault scenario occur. Further, a critical transient voltage sag
is predicted to occur for fault scenario 2031–2032 and 4031–4032. Whereas, fault scenario
1042–1045, 1042–1044, 4032–4042, 4042–4043, 4042–4044 and 4043–4044 are predicted to
have a near-critical transient voltage sag. For fault scenario 4042-4044 a transient voltage
sag is predicted to occur at load bus 43 and 46, both primarily depressed by the generator at
bus 4021. However, notice the total change in the bus voltage is low, indicating the voltage
has recovered to a low voltage after fault clearance. The results obtained for fault scenario
1042–1044 and 1042–1045 are identical to event 1 and will not be investigated in further
detail during event 2.
Comparing event 1, where the PMU snapshot is obtained in a steady-state, with event
2 where the PMU snapshot is assumed to be obtained in a quasi steady-state does in fact
indicate that the power system is in a quasi steady-state, since the method produces similar
results for the two events. However, an exact match is not expected since line 4046–4047 is
out of service in event 2, which means the grid topology is different between the two events.

bus 2031 is predicted to reach a minimum voltage magnitude of 0.70 pu once GEN-7 reaches

Comparing the TDS results with the results obtained from the method, it is seen that
load bus 2031 reaches a minimum voltage magnitude of 0.77 pu at 0.50 s during the TDS
as seen in Fig. 4.27, which matches with GEN-7 reaching its return angle at 0.50 s as seen
in Fig. 4.28. Moreover, the actual transient voltage sag experienced by load bus 2031 cor-
responds to a near-critical voltage sag, and not a critical voltage sag as predicted by the
method. This difference is suspected to be caused by the increase in the field voltage of
GEN-7 at bus 2032, affecting both bus 2032 and 2031, similar to event 1. The transient
voltage sag is not as severe as for event 1, which could be due to the topological changes
in event 2 with line 4046–4047 out of service. Further, as seen from Fig. 4.28 GEN-7 is the
only generator accelerating relative to the other generators, which matches the candidate of
interest, candidate 0. In addition, once E-SIME declared candidate 0 first-swing transient
stable at time 0.22 s, the rotor angle of GEN-7 with respect to the COI was 34.5◦. According
to the method GEN-7 should reach its return angle at δr = 34.5◦+ 45.8◦= 80.3◦, which is
0.3◦above the actual return angle at 80◦. Similar to event 1 the predicted return angle is
close to the actual return angle, however, the increase in GEN-7’s field voltage is likely the
reason the predicted minimum is not reached at bus 2031.

==PAGE:110==
4.3 Preventive control actions
96
4.3
Preventive control actions
Weckesser et al. in [17] found that a reduction in the active power output of the critical
generator, and redispatching this amount to other non-critical generators, resulted in a
reduced rotor angle excursion and hence a less severe transient voltage sag at the load bus.
This is utilized to investigate its impact on the transient voltage sag observed at load bus
2031 for fault scenario 2031–2032.
The pre-fault active power output at GEN-7, is reduced by 50 MW and redispatched to
GEN-3, which implies the mechanical power is reduced at GEN-7 and increased at GEN-3
with this amount. GEN-3 was chosen since it is located close to GEN-7 and its impact on
the transient voltage sag compared to GEN-7 is negligible as seen in Fig. 4.5. The impact
of the redispatch on the transient voltage sag is seen in Fig. 4.37. The transient voltage
sag has become less severe and is no longer close to the critical voltage of 0.70 pu. After
the redispatch the voltage sag’s new minimum is around 0.81 pu, and the duration of the
transient voltage sag has been reduced. As seen from Fig. 4.38 GEN-7 reaches its return angle
earlier and at a lower rotor angle. Furthermore, after the return angle has been reached,
the rotor angle also decreases at a faster rate compared to when the 50 MW has not been
redispatched to GEN-3.
Due to the mechanical power being reduced the generator cannot accelerate by the same
amount during the fault, since the mismatch between the mechanical and electrical power
is reduced. Hence, when the fault is cleared, the generator requires a smaller deceleration
area to expend the gained kinetic energy, resulting in the return angle being reached at a
lower rotor angle. Furthermore, reducing the mechanical power, causes the unstable rotor
angle δu to be located at a higher rotor angle, which increases the available deceleration area.
This implies the generator can withstand a longer fault duration before it loses synchronism.
Also, since the generator reaches the return angle sooner and at a rotor angle which is further
away from δu, the duration of the transient voltage sag is reduced. When the return angle
is close to δu the available negative acceleration power is close to zero, which implies a slow
deceleration in the rotor speed and hence slow reduction in rotor angle. However, when the
return angle is further away from δu the available negative acceleration power at the return
angle is larger and results in an increased deceleration in the rotor speed and hence increased
reduction in the rotor angle.

4.3 Preventive control actions
97

CHAPTER 5
Discussion
The method has proved to be able to screen through a list of fault scenarios and highlight
the ones of interest. However, the validation of the method also showed that the severity
of the transient voltage sags did not always match with the actual voltage sag observed.
The method has been able to correctly identify the fault scenarios where a transient voltage
sag truly occurs, such as fault scenario 2031–2032 and 1013–1014. However, for both fault
scenarios the voltage magnitude at the load bus decreases after fault clearance, and the rotor
angle evolutions for the generators are not fully grouped up. Moreover, for fault scenario
2031–2032 it was observed that GEN-7’s AVR at bus 2032 affected the voltage magnitude at
bus 2031, which caused the predicted minimum voltage magnitude of bus 2031 to be slightly
lower than the actual minimum voltage magnitude, since the method did not take the effect
of GEN-7’s AVR into account. However, the method is supposed to provide a conservative
result, which it has accomplished by not considering the effect of GEN-7’s AVR and using
the candidate which results in the lowest voltage magnitude during the transient voltage
sag at load bus 2031. Furthermore, the objective of the method is not to replace the grid
operator, but instead be used as a supportive tool, which enables the grid operator to focus
more time and effort on the fault scenarios which according to the method pose a threat to
the power system.
The method has been able to identify all the fault scenarios, which truly cause the
power system to become first-swing transient unstable.
Though, it has falsely identified
some fault scenarios as first-swing transient unstable. This can be solved by a more strict
threshold of e.g., Γ < 1 %. However, this would increase the run-time of E-SIME for all
fault scenarios, implying that for fault scenarios experiencing a transient voltage sag, E-
SIME would drive the TDS past the occurrence of the transient voltage sag, preventing the
method from predicting the minimum of the transient voltage sag. Therefore, in order to
achieve an acceptable trade-off Γ < 20 % is used, which ensures the method is able to predict
the occurrence of transient voltage sags, however, at the expense that some fault scenarios
are falsely identified as first-swing transient unstable.
For some of the fault scenarios the method falsely identified transient voltage sags, how-
ever, this was due to the identified candidates and their number of CMs. These candidates
reached their return angle during the E-SIME assessment, and due to the voltage magnitude
at some of these load buses recovering at a voltage below 0.90 pu the method determined the
minimum of a transient voltage sag had been reached. However, as was seen from the TDS
a transient voltage sag never occurred, and the voltage magnitude continued to increase
after fault clearance. Hence, the method could be improved by considering the slope of the
voltage magnitudes based on the TDS data obtained while E-SIME is running. Thus, if all
the candidates have reached their return angle during the E-SIME assessment, it can be
checked whether any of the load bus voltage magnitudes have decreased after fault clear-
ance, which could indicate that a minimum has been reached. This solution would catch
fault scenarios such as 1041–1043 where there did not occur a transient voltage sag. How-
ever, it would falsely conclude that there occurs no transient voltage sag for fault scenario
1042–1044 and 1042–1045. The candidates for these two fault scenarios also reached their
==PAGE:113==
5 Discussion
99
return angle during the E-SIME assessment, and the voltage magnitudes of the load buses
were all increasing initially. However, load bus 142 did experience a transient voltage sag.
The true candidate for this fault scenario is a single CM, which is GEN-5. However, the
technique used to identify the cluster of CMs is unable to isolate GEN-5, since its rotor
angle evolution is grouped with the other generators. Thus, a solution for this issue may
be to consider the slope of each generator’s rotor angle evolution, which would for this case
have been able to isolate GEN-5 from the rest. Hence, the correct return angle of GEN-5
would have been predicted, enabling the prediction of the minimum of the transient voltage
sag at bus 142.
Moreover, the preventive control action of reducing the pre-fault active power of the
critical generator, and redispatching this amount to non-critical generators proved to reduce
the severity of the transient voltage sag.
Therefore, the method should be improved by
including countermeasures based on this kind of preventive control action. This could be
achieved by reducing the active power output of the critical generator by a certain amount,
and redispatching this amount to a selected number of generators, chosen based on their
sensitivity to the transient voltage sag. These generators should either contribute positively
to the voltage sag or have a negligible negative contribution. Further, the MVA rating and
how loaded these selected generators are also needs to be considered. Thus, by performing
a new TDS based on a power flow using the same PMU snapshot where the redispatching
changes have been made, it is possible to determine if the severity of the transient voltage
sag has been reduced. Hence, when the method has determined the severity of the tran-
sient voltage sag has been reduced, the grid operator can be provided with the appropriate
preventive control actions.
Lastly, the run-time of the method was around 36 −37 s, which for larger power systems
will become longer, however, by utilizing several central processing unit (CPU) cores the
run-time can be significantly improved. The list of fault scenarios can be divided between
a given number of CPU cores, which would enable the screening of the fault scenarios in
parallel. This would result in an improved real-time assessment of the power system since
the method will be able to perform its assessment on a new PMU snapshot faster.
==PAGE:114==
CHAPTER 6
Conclusion
This thesis concerned the implementation and validation of a method able to assess transient
voltage sags occurring due to large generator rotor swings, following a transient disturbance
in an electric power system. The overall objective has been successfully achieved, by imple-
menting a method able to automatically predict the occurrence of transient voltage sags and
pin-point their location in the power system.
• How can transient stability of a power system with multiple generators be assessed,
and if transient stable how can a transient voltage sag be predicted?
The E-SIME method has successfully been implemented and is able to perform a first-
swing TSA of a multimachine system. E-SIME was able to identify all the cases which
resulted in a first-swing transient unstable system. However, due to the complexity
of the rotor angle evolutions, E-SIME falsely declared some of the cases first-swing
transient unstable.
For the transient stable cases, the return angle of each OMIB
candidate was predicted, and the candidate resulting in the lowest voltage minimum
during the transient voltage sag was used to ensure a conservative result was obtained.
The method was able to identify the cases where a severe transient voltage sag would
occur, however, for some cases where the voltage magnitudes recovered slowly after
fault clearance, the method falsely predicted that a transient voltage sag would occur.
For fault scenario 2031–2032 during event 1 the predicted return angle of the single
critical machine, GEN-7 deviated with 1.7◦from the actual return angle. Whereas,
for event 2 it deviated with 0.3◦. However, despite the accurately predicted return
angle of GEN-7, the predicted voltage magnitude minimum of the transient voltage
sag at load bus 2031 was lower than the actual minimum. This was suspected to be
due to GEN-7’s AVR counteracting the transient voltage sag at load bus 2031.
• How can a screening mechanism be developed, which automatically pin-points critical
contingencies, their location and the contribution of each generator to the transient
voltage sag in the power system?
The screening mechanism has been successfully implemented, which automatically
screens through a list of fault scenarios and performs a TSA and TVSA for each. For
the first-swing transient stable fault scenarios, the minimum of the transient voltage
sag is predicted from where the individual contribution to the voltage sag of each
generator is obtained, and the generator contributing most negatively is highlighted.
The results obtained from the TSA and TVSA are stored by the screening mechanism
allowing the results to be further processed.
• How can this screening mechanism be used to monitor a power system in real-time?
The method has been coupled with the WARTApp platform, which allowed for assess-
ing the power system in real-time utilizing PMU snapshots. Each PMU snapshot was
used to initialize the models in the TDS tool, in order to achieve the correct operating
point for the TDS. Furthermore, the method was able to assess a system which had
experienced a transient disturbance, however, the PMU snapshot had to be at least
in a quasi steady-state in order to initialize the models in the TDS tool. This was
==PAGE:115==
6.1 Future work
101
achieved by utilizing the change in each generator’s internal voltage magnitude with
respect to time. The system was deemed to be in a quasi steady-state if the changes
were below 0.01 pu/s. Further, the run-time of the method for each PMU snapshot
was between 36 −37 s.
• What type of preventive control action can be performed in order to prevent the occur-
rence of the predicted transient voltage sag, in case the critical contingency occurs in
the power system?
The effect of lowering the pre-fault active power output of the critical generator and
redispatching this amount to non-critical generators proved to reduce the severity
of the transient voltage sag. This was demonstrated with a fault scenario where a
severe transient voltage sag had been observed, and after the redispatch the severity
of the transient voltage sag had been significantly reduced. The reduction in the pre-
fault active power corresponds to reducing the mechanical power, which implies the
critical generator gains less kinetic energy during the fault period, since the mismatch
between the mechanical and electrical power is less. Hence, this results in the generator
reaching its return angle at a lower rotor angle. The fact that it reached its return
angle at a lower rotor angle resulted in a larger negative acceleration power being
available, causing the rotor speed to decrease at a higher rate, implying the rotor
angle decreases faster, reducing the duration of the transient voltage sag.
• How can an operator of the power system be alerted by these predicted critical contin-
gencies in order to ensure system awareness?
The results obtained from the TSA and TVSA have been visualized in a table and im-
proves the grid operator’s system awareness, by highlighting the fault scenarios which
may pose a threat to the power system. In case a system becomes transient unstable
it is highlighted by the color red in the TSA column. Whereas, if the system is tran-
sient stable, the operator is presented with four different colors in the TVSA column:
green, orange, yellow and red with yellow being near-critical and red being a critical
transient voltage sag. The grid operator is able to quickly assess the severity of each
fault scenario from the different colors. However, in order to focus the attention of
the grid operator to the fault scenarios experiencing a near-critical and critical tran-
sient voltage sag, the detailed information about the voltage sag’s minimum, location
and contribution by the critical generator is only provided for these scenarios. The
method and hence the visualization of its assessment has been designed in order to
complement the grid operator, by improving system awareness and enabling the grid
operator to focus more time and effort on investigating the critical and near-critical
fault scenarios highlighted by the method.
6.1
Future work
In the following an outlook on future work is presented.
• Determine appropriate preventive control action: The method could be ex-
tended in order to estimate the necessary reduction in the pre-fault active power
output for the critical generator, and identify which non-critical generators should be
redispatched with this amount.
• Investigate other types of voltage sags: The method could be extended to handle
other types of voltage sags caused by events such as, load demand changes possibly
==PAGE:116==
6.1 Future work
102
causing a voltage sag at a load bus due to the increase in current flow in the transmis-
sion lines.
• Investigate the impact of unbalanced transmission line faults on the sever-
ity of the transient voltage sag: The impact of unbalanced transmission line faults
could be investigated by utilizing sequence components, which would further improve
the grid operator’s system awareness.
• Determine how much the fault duration can be extended before reaching
the unstable angle: This can be used to obtain a margin, which indicates how close
the power system is at becoming first-swing transient unstable.

APPENDIX F
Python Code
F.1
Implementation of TDS tool
F.1.1
Main control of the TDS tool

1
'''
2
3
Main file used to run the time-domain simulation tool
4
5
Ensure the correct path for the raw and dyr-file is set.
6
7
OMIB is used to toggle E-SIME for the TSA.
8
if OMIB = False: Normal TDS is performed
9
if OMIB = True: E-SIME controls the TDS in order to perform the TSA,
which is used to perform the TVSA.
10
11
ld.Ybus contains the Ybus matrix used for the power flow.
12
Ybus_updated_normal is the augmented admittance matrix used during the
dynamical simulation.
13
14
Pe and Pm is on the individual machine's MVA base
15
16
'''
17
18
import Loaddyn

F.1.2
Load network data and construction of the admittance
matrix

1
"""
2
This file imports data from the raw-file and constructs the Ybus matrix
used to perform
3
the power flow to obtain the steady-state bus voltages.
4
5
Sbus contains the power injected into a given bus in the system. If
there both is production and consumption at a bus,
6
then these two contributions are added together. Generator convention is
used.
7
8
V0 is indexed after the busNumbers vector, so V0 and Ybus share the same
indices
9
10
In the raw file the shunts are based on the generator convention , so Q >
0 means they are producing Mvar and
11
Q < 0 means they are absorbing Mvar. When converting the shunts to
impedances then their Mvar value has to be
12
provided using the load convetion, obtained by multiplying each Mvar
value with -1. This will ensure that the correct impedance value
13
is obtained for capacitors and inductors.
14
15
For the power flow calculation the following is included in the
admittance matrix:
16
Lines
17
leakage reactance of transformers
18
shunts (e.g., capacitor banks or reactor banks). The shunts are provided
on the generator convention in the raw-file
19
==PAGE:179==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
165
20
# bus codes:
21
# 1 = load(PQ) bus (P and Q are specified)
22
# 2 = generator(PV) bus (P and V are specified)
23
# 3 = reference(slack) bus (V = 1.0 pu and phsae = 0 deg is specified) -
this bus will balance the power flow, by providing the remaining
needed power for the power flow to converge.
24
25
"""
26
27

"""
2
This file contains the powerflow algorithm and is based on the Newton-
Raphson algorithm.
3
4
V0 contains the steady-state voltages obtained from the power flow.
5
6
V_start contains the voltages from the PV-buses and ref-bus. For PQ-
buses the voltage is initially set to be 1 pu.
7
8
Sbus contains the known P and Q injections into the bus for both PV, and
PQ buses.
9
"""

import copy

import sys

def Q_limit(Ybus,V0,Sbus,pv_index,pq_index,busNumbers,PVbusload,
pv_old_index_store ,V_start,Qmax,Qmin,pv_index_norm , busGenNumbers ,
ref):

92

estimate of the reactive power injection for all generators
based on the estimated complex bus voltages

95

then Q_geninj is obtained (Qgen = Qinj - Qload)

def generate_Derivatives(Ybus,V0):
223
224
J_ds_dVm = np.diag(V0/np.absolute(V0)).dot(np.diag((Ybus.dot(V0))).
conj()) + \
225
np.diag(V0).dot(Ybus.dot(np.diag(V0/np.absolute(V0))).conj())
226

diag(V0))).conj())
228
229
return J_ds_dVm,J_dS_dTheta

def generate_Jacobian(J_dS_dVm,J_dS_dTheta ,pv_index,pq_index):
233
234
# Append PV and PQ indices for convenience
235
pvpq_ind = np.append(pv_index, pq_index)
236
237
# Create the sub-matrices
238
J_11 = np.real(J_dS_dTheta[np.ix_(pvpq_ind, pvpq_ind)])
239
J_12 = np.real(J_dS_dVm[np.ix_(pvpq_ind, pq_index)])
240
J_21 = np.imag(J_dS_dTheta[np.ix_(pq_index, pvpq_ind)])
241
J_22 = np.imag(J_dS_dVm[np.ix_(pq_index, pq_index)])
242
243
J = np.block([[J_11,J_12],[J_21,J_22]])
244
245
return J

"""

This file updates the Ybus matrix by adding each of the loads to the

3

F.1.9
Solve algebraic network equations

1
'''
2
Solving for algebraic network equations to obtain the complex bus
voltages during the dynamical simulation.
==PAGE:207==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
193
3
4
The generator current, Ig from the current source is used as the current
injected into the bus (where Yg is connected),
5
where Yg = 1/Xd_tr and since Yg is included in the Ybus matrix.
6
The voltage from V = I/Y will be the voltage on the terminals of the
generator.
7
8
However, note Ig is not the stator current Ia, but Ia = Ig-Ig_y where
Ig_y is the current flowing into the admittance , Yg.
9
Hence the stator current is then the remaining part flowing into the
connected system when using a Norton equivalent , where Ig is the
Norton current.
10
11
'''
12
13
import numpy as np
14
import scipy.linalg
15
16
def UpdatebusVoltages(Xgen, Y, Py, Ly, Uy, busNumbers, busNumbers_dyn ,
ngens, Pgenrou, Pgensal ,nbus, GDMVA, genroubusIndex , gensalbusIndex
,Sbase, t):

Psid_tr = Xgensal[3]

Xd_tr = Pgensal[2]

Xd_subtr = Pgensal[3]

Xl = Pgensal[4]

Xd_subtr)/(Xd_tr-Xl)
37

voltage on stator

# Calculating generator current

dividing
the Thevenin voltage (E) with the Thevenin

shifting its phase to be in accordance with the synchrous

F.1 Implementation of TDS tool

rotating frame

Xd_subtr)/(Xq_tr-Xl))

Xd_subtr)/(Xd_tr-Xl)

71
72
Igen[genroubusIndex] = I_netref_Sbase
73
74
75
# Igen is ordered according to busNumbers_dyn
76
# The generator currents from Igen has to be stored at the correct
indexes in Ig to match the correct generator.
77
# Ig is ordered according to busNumbers , and hence contains a
current injection at each bus in the system.
78
for i, busName in enumerate(busNumbers):
79
ind = np.where(busNumbers_dyn == busName)[0] # gives the index
where busName is located in busNumbers_dyn
80
if np.size(ind) == 0:
81
continue
82
else:
83
if len(ind) == 1:
84
ind = ind[0]
85
Ig[i] = Igen[ind]
==PAGE:209==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
195
86
87
elif len(ind) > 1:
88
for IND in ind:
89
Ig[i] += Igen[IND] # adding the two generator
currents to find the total current injected into
the bus
90
91
Ig = np.transpose(np.array([Ig]))
92
93
# Updating the system voltages, U at each bus in the system (using
the LU-factorization of the admittance matrix)
94
95
U = (scipy.linalg.inv(Py@Ly@Uy)@Ig).reshape(1,-1)[0]
96
97
return U

'''
2
3
Compute the differential of the states for GENROU
4
5
'''
6
7
8
import numpy as np
9
10
def dXgen_GENROU(Xgen, Vgen, Efd, Pm, Params, a, b, t):
11
omega = Xgen[1]
12
Eq_tr = Xgen[2]
13
Ed_tr = Xgen[3]
14
Psiq_tr = Xgen[4]
15
Psid_tr = Xgen[5]
16
omega_0 = np.ones([len(Xgen[1])]) # synchronous speed of the rotor
in per-unit
17
18
Xd = Params[0]
19
Xq = Params[1]
20
Xd_tr = Params[2]
21
Xq_tr = Params[3]
22
Xd_subtr = Params[4]
23
Xl = Params[5]
24
Td0_tr = Params[6]
25
Tq0_tr = Params[7]
26
Td0_subtr = Params[8]
27
Tq0_subtr = Params[9]
28
H = Params[10]
29
D = Params[11]
30
Xq_subtr = Xd_subtr # neglecting subtransient saliency
==PAGE:210==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
196
31
32
# omega and omega_s is the speed and synchronous speed of the rotor.
33
omega_s = 2*np.pi*50 # synchronous speed base on the stator side in
rad/s - used to obtain the derivative of the rotor angle on the
stator side to obtain the rotor angle on the stator side when
integrating ddelta
34
35
Id = Vgen[0,:]
36
Iq = Vgen[1,:]
37
Te = Vgen[2,:]
38
39
Psiq_subtr = -(Ed_tr * (Xq_subtr - Xl)/(Xq_tr - Xl) + Psiq_tr*(Xq_tr
- Xq_subtr)/(Xq_tr - Xl))
40
Psid_subtr = Eq_tr * (Xd_subtr - Xl)/(Xd_tr - Xl) + Psid_tr*(Xd_tr -
Xd_subtr)/(Xd_tr - Xl)
41
42
Psid_abs = np.sqrt(Psid_subtr**2+Psiq_subtr**2)
43
44
Sat = (b*(Psid_abs-a)**2)/Psid_abs
45
46
# Establishing the differential equations (with saturation)
47
ddelta = (omega - omega_0)*omega_s
48
domega = (1/(2*H) * ((-D * (omega - omega_0) + Pm)/omega - Te)) #
domega is in per-unit, so [p.u./s]
49
dEq_tr = (1/Td0_tr)*(Efd-(Eq_tr+(Xd-Xd_tr)*(Id+((Xd_tr-Xd_subtr)/((
Xd_tr-Xl)**2))*(Eq_tr-Psid_tr -(Xd_tr-Xl)*Id))+Psid_subtr*Sat))
50
dEd_tr = (1/Tq0_tr)*(-Ed_tr+(Psiq_subtr*Sat)*(Xq-Xl)/(Xd-Xl)+(Xq-
Xq_tr)*(Iq-((Xq_tr-Xq_subtr)/((Xq_tr-Xl)**2))*(Ed_tr-Psiq_tr+Iq
*(Xq_tr-Xl))))
51
dPsiq_tr = (1/Tq0_subtr)*(-Psiq_tr+Ed_tr+Iq*(Xq_tr-Xl))
52
dPsid_tr = (1/Td0_subtr)*(-Psid_tr+Eq_tr-Id*(Xd_tr-Xl))
53
54
55
dF = np.vstack((ddelta,domega,dEq_tr,dEd_tr,dPsiq_tr,dPsid_tr))
56
57
# if values are below 1e-5 then they are assumed to be 0.
58
59
if (abs(dF) < 1e-5).all():
60
dF[:] = 0
61
62
63
return dF

Algebraic equations for generators

'''
2
Computing the machine currents in dq-frame, and the electrical torque of
each generator.
3
'''
4
5
import numpy as np
6
7
def UpdateMachineCurrents(Xgen,U,ngens,Pgenrou,Pgensal,genroubusIndex ,
gensalbusIndex):
8
9
10
Id = np.zeros(ngens,dtype=np.float)
11
Iq = np.zeros(ngens,dtype=np.float)
12
Te = np.zeros(ngens,dtype=np.float)
13
Vinternal = np.zeros(ngens,dtype=np.float)
14
15
16
if Pgenrou is not None:
17
Xgenrou = Xgen[0]
18
19
delta = Xgenrou[0]
20
omega = Xgenrou[1]
21
Psiq_tr = Xgenrou[4]
22
Psid_tr = Xgenrou[5]
23
Eq_tr = Xgenrou[2]
24
Ed_tr = Xgenrou[3]
25
Xd_tr = Pgenrou[2]
26
Xq_tr = Pgenrou[3]
27
Xd_subtr = Pgenrou[4]
28
Xl = Pgenrou[5]
29
30
Vdq = U[genroubusIndex]*np.exp(1j*(-delta+np.pi/2)) # Voltage at
the terminals converted from DQ-reference frame to
indivudal generator's dq-reference frame (Vq = np.imag(Vdq)
and Vd = np.real(Vdq) )
31
32
Psiq_subtr = -(Ed_tr*(Xd_subtr-Xl)/(Xq_tr-Xl)+Psiq_tr*(Xq_tr-
Xd_subtr)/(Xq_tr-Xl))
33
Psid_subtr = Eq_tr*(Xd_subtr-Xl)/(Xd_tr-Xl)+Psid_tr*(Xd_tr-
Xd_subtr)/(Xd_tr-Xl)
34
==PAGE:224==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
210
35
E = (-Psiq_subtr+1j*Psid_subtr)*omega
# Calculating internal
voltage on stator
36
37
Idq = (E-Vdq)/(1j*Xd_subtr)
# Calculating current flowing in
the stator
38
39
Id[genroubusIndex] = np.real(Idq) # id = (Psid_subtr*omega-Vq)/
Xd_subtr
40
Iq[genroubusIndex] = np.imag(Idq) # iq = (Psiq_subtr*omega+Vd)/
Xd_subtr (would be Xq_subtr for calculation of iq if sub-
transient saliency is not neglected)
41
Vinternal[genroubusIndex] = np.abs(E)
42
43
44
Te[genroubusIndex] = Psid_subtr*Iq[genroubusIndex] - Psiq_subtr*
Id[genroubusIndex]
45
46
if Pgensal is not None:
47
Xgensal = Xgen[1]
48
49
delta = Xgensal[0]
50
omega = Xgensal[1]
51
Psiq_subtr = Xgensal[4]
52
Psid_tr = Xgensal[3]
53
Eq_tr = Xgensal[2]
54
Xd_tr = Pgensal[2]
55
Xd_subtr = Pgensal[3]
56
Xl = Pgensal[4]
57
58
Psid_subtr = Eq_tr*(Xd_subtr-Xl)/(Xd_tr-Xl)+Psid_tr*(Xd_tr-
Xd_subtr)/(Xd_tr-Xl)
59
60
Vdq = U[gensalbusIndex]*np.exp(1j*(-delta+np.pi/2))
61
62
E = (-Psiq_subtr+1j*Psid_subtr)*omega
# Calculating internal
voltage on stator (Ed_subtr = -Psiq_subtr and Eq_subtr =
Psid_subtr) -> E = (Ed_subtr + j*Eq_subtr)
63
64
Idq = (E-Vdq)/(1j*Xd_subtr) # Calculating current flowing in the
stator
65
66
Id[gensalbusIndex] = np.real(Idq)
67
Iq[gensalbusIndex] = np.imag(Idq)
68
Vinternal[gensalbusIndex] = np.abs(E)
69
70
Te[gensalbusIndex] = (Psid_subtr*Iq[gensalbusIndex] - Psiq_subtr
*Id[gensalbusIndex])
71
72
73
Vgen = np.vstack((Id, Iq, Te, Vinternal))
74
75
==PAGE:225==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
211
76
return Vgen

'''
2
3
Compute the differential of the states for TGOV1
4
5
The reference power, Pref is the mechanical power in steady-state.
6
'''
7
8
import numpy as np
9
10
11
12
def dXT1gov(Xgov,omega,Pref,Params):
13
R = Params[0]
14
T1 = Params[1]
15
T2 = Params[2]
16
T3 = Params[3]
17
Vmax = Params[4]
18
Vmin = Params[5]
19
20
g = Xgov[0]
21
p = Xgov[1]
22
23
omega_s = np.ones(len(g))
24
25
# governor state-equations
26
dg = 1/T1*(Pref-1/R*(omega-omega_s))-1/T1*g
27
dp = T2/(T1*T3)*(Pref-1/R*(omega-omega_s))+(1/T3-T2/(T1*T3))*g-1/T3*
p
28
29
30
# The non-windup of the time-constant block for state, g:
31
# when T2 = T3 lead/lag block is neglected and p = g.
32
for j, e_val in enumerate(g):
33
if e_val >= Vmax[j] and dg[j] > 0:
34
dg[j] = 0
35
# g[j] = Vmax[j] # Included in the integration main
36
if T2[j] == T3[j]: # lead/lag block is neglected, so g = p,
implying dg = dp.
37
dp[j] = 0
38
# p[j] = Vmax[j] # Included in the integration main
39
40
elif e_val <= Vmin[j] and dg[j] < 0:
41
dg[j] = 0
42
# g[j] = Vmin[j] # Included in the integration main
43
if T2[j] == T3[j]:
==PAGE:226==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
212
44
dp[j] = 0
45
#p[j] = Vmin[j] # Included in the integration main
46
47
48
49
dF = np.vstack((dg,dp))
50
51
if (abs(dF[:]) < 1e-6).all():
52
dF[:] = 0
53
54
return dF

'''

3
Compute the differential of the states for HYGOV
4
5

6
7
import numpy as np
8
9
def dXgov(Xgov,omega,Params,nref):
10
omega_s = np.ones(len(Xgov[0,:]))
11
==PAGE:227==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
213
12
R = Params[0]
13
r = Params[1]
14
Tr = Params[2]
15
Tf = Params[3]
16
Tg = Params[4]
17
Tw = Params[5]
18
GMAX = Params[10]
19
GMIN = Params[11]
20
VELM = Params[9]
21
22
g = Xgov[0,:]
23
q = Xgov[1,:]
24
c = Xgov[2,:]
25
e = Xgov[3,:]
26
d = nref-(omega-omega_s+c*R) # error term
27
28
# governor and turbine state-equations
29
de = -1/Tf*e+1/Tf*d
30
dc = (1/(r*Tr)-1/(r*Tf))*e + 1/(r*Tf)*d
31
dg = -1/Tg*g+1/Tg*c
32
dq = (1-(q/g)**2)*1/Tw
33
34
35
# Non-windup limit applied to the desired gate opening
36
for j, c_val in enumerate(c):
37
if c_val >= GMAX[j] and dc[j] > 0:
38
dc[j] = 0
39
# c[j] = GMAX[j] set in the integration main script
40
41
elif c_val <= GMIN[j] and dc[j] < 0:
42
dc[j] = 0
43
# c[j] = GMIN[j] set in the integration main script
44
45
# Velocity limit for the desired gate opening (dc is the velocity of
the gate, and it cannot exceed VELM)
46
for j, c_val in enumerate(c):
47
if abs(dc[j]) > VELM[j]:
48
if dc[j] < 0: # gate is closing
49
dc[j] = -VELM[j]
50
else: # gate is opening
51
dc[j] = VELM[j]
52
53
54
dF = np.vstack((dg, dq, dc, de))
55
56
if (abs(dF) < 1e-5).all():
57
dF[:] = 0
58
59
60
return dF

def dXexc(Xexc,Vref,V,VST,Params):

14

Tb = Params[1]
==PAGE:229==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
215
16

17

18

19
Emin = Params[5]
20

23

24

and c as output
28

main numerical integrator loop

35
dc[j] = 0

main numerical integrator loop
37

41
dF[:] = 0

a = K2*T2*dv0
30
31
dv1 = 1/T2*(a-v1)
32
b = K2*T2*dv1
33
34
dv2 = 1/T2*(b-v2)
35
c = K2*T2*dv2
36
37
dd = 1/T3*(K3*c-d)
38
de = 1/T5*(K5*(d+K4*c)-e)
39
df = 1/T5*(K5*e-f)
==PAGE:231==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
217
40
41
dF = np.vstack((dv0,dv1,dv2,dd,de,df))
42
43
if (abs(dF) < 1e-5).all():
44
dF[:] = 0
45
46
return dF

'''

values at t = t+1.
3
Current integrator in use: Modified Euler
4
5

, multiplies it with stepsize, to find the
==PAGE:232==
F.1 Implementation of TDS tool
218
6

given by x1 = x0 + dx0/dt*stepsize
7

8

Construction of the load voltage sensitivity matrix

1
'''
2
3
Computes the load sensitivity matrix with dimensions , num_load x
num_gens
4
5
Each entry gives the l-th load bus' voltage magnitude change for 1 rad

6
7
Each column in SVG corresponds to a given generator

Each row in SVG corresponds to a given load bus

def LVsens(ngens,nloadbus,Zloadindex,Vtc,Xgen, Py, Ly, Uy, busNumbers,

19

i-th generator's internal complex voltage with 1 degree.
20
Y = scipy.linalg.inv(Py @ Ly @ Uy)

21
22
23
for k in range(ngens):

25

26

voltage will be shifted with epsilon

and GENSAL)

Psid_tr * (Xd_tr - Xd_subtr) / (Xd_tr - Xl)

54

Psid_tr * (Xd_tr - Xd_subtr) / (Xd_tr - Xl)

F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA

F.2.2
Contribution of each generator to transient voltage sag

1
'''
2
==PAGE:256==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA
242
3
Calculates how much each generator is contributing in the change in the
load bus' voltage.
4
5
dVactual is a numloads x numGens matrix.
6
Each column contains the contribution to the load bus' change by each
generator.
7
The sum of all the columns for a given row index will give the total
voltage change at this load bus with the given row index.
8
9
f is a numloads x numGens matrix.
10
This matrix contains the fraction of how much each generator is
contribution to the voltage change at a particular load bus.
11
f is used to calculate the actual change in voltage each generator is
contributing with, which results in dVactual.
12
13
14
V_tc = voltage right after clearing
15
d_tc = rotor angle right after clearing
16
V_min = Minimum voltage during the transient voltage sag
17
d_tVmin = rotor angle at the time when the minimum voltage is found
18
'''
19
20
21
import numpy as np
22
23
def GVsens(SVG,d_tc,d_tVmin,V_tc,V_min):
24
25
numLoads = len(SVG[:,0])
26
numGens = len(SVG[0,:])
27
28
dV_total = np.zeros(numLoads) # number of loads
29
ddelta = d_tVmin-d_tc # (d_tVmin = rotor angle of each generator
when the minimum voltage is observed. d_tc = rotor angle right
after clearing the event)
30
31
# each column in SVG corresponds to a generator
32
# each row in SVG corresponds to a load bus
33
f = np.zeros((numLoads,numGens))
34
35
for i in range(numLoads):
36
# calculating the contribution to the voltage change for each
generator len(SVG[i,:]) = len(ddelta), so SVG[i,0] is the
sensitivty to load bus i for generator 0, and ddelta[0]
contains the rotor angle change for generator 0
37
dV_total[i] = np.sum(SVG[i,:]*ddelta) # contains the total
voltage change at load bus i
38
39
40
for i in range(numLoads):
41
42
f[i,:] = (SVG[i,:]*ddelta)/np.abs(dV_total[i])
43
==PAGE:257==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA
243
44
45
dV_contribution_from_each_gen = np.zeros((numLoads,numGens))
46
47
for i in range(numLoads):
48
49
dV_contribution_from_each_gen[i,:] = f[i,:]*np.abs((np.abs(V_tc[
i])-np.abs(V_min[i]))) # contains the contribution from each
generator to the voltage change at load bus i
50
51
return dV_contribution_from_each_gen

def convertHtoM(self): # Following converts H to M where M is in p.u
. with units s^2/rad
58

base which is the rated synchronous speed on the stator side

def OMIB_at_t_i(self, delta, omega, Pe, Pm, clusterInd):

def computeStability(self, d_OMIB_i, Pa_OMIB_i, w_OMIB_i, M_OMIB,
eta_old, a, b, c, OMIB_id,t):
123
124
error = np.nan
125
eta = np.nan
126
127
if w_OMIB_i < 0:
128
self.status_OMIB[OMIB_id] = 2
129
print('Rotor speed has passed below zero, OMIB is on its
return path.')

print('Return angle is set equal to this current rotor angle
of the OMIB, which will be slightly lower than the
actual return angle of this OMIB.\n')

132

133

to be >= 0 for there to be a real solution

# by checking if dPa > 0 at delta = delta_u, then we
ensure that the fit for the Pa-delta curve is
correct and has a U shape. Ensuring that dPa > 0 at
delta_u means the slope of the Pa-delta curve is
positive at delta_u.
151
# Below delta_u Pa < 0 and above it is Pa > 0, and at
delta_u, Pa = 0, so by ensuring dPa > 0, that will
be ensured. If dPa >! 0, then the obtained Pa-delta
curve is not physically correct.
152
# We know that when delta_u is crossed, then Pe goes
below Pm (Pe < Pm), meaning Pa > 0, causing the
machine to accelerate and lose synchronism.
153
# However, below delta_u, Pe is above Pm (Pe > Pm) and
the machine is decelerating , meaning Pa < 0.
154

# derivative of Pa with respect to
delta. dPa gives the derivative of Pa at delta = d_u
155

==PAGE:261==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA
247
157
158

margin is only calculated if d_i < d_u

the inertia, M, then eta = [rad^2/s^2]

d_OMIB_i**2)/2+c*(d_u-d_OMIB_i))-1/2*M_OMIB*
w_OMIB_i**2

OMIB candidate: ',OMIB_id);print('\n

180
181
return eta_old,error,eta
182
183
184
185
def ComputeReturnAngle(self, d_OMIB_i, w_OMIB_i,M_OMIB_i, a, b, c,
OMIB_id):
186
187
def return_angle(d_r):
188
return -(a*(-d_OMIB_i**3+d_r**3)/3+b*(-d_OMIB_i**2+d_r**2)
/2+c*(d_r-d_OMIB_i))-1/2*M_OMIB_i*w_OMIB_i**2
189
190
sol = root(return_angle , x0=np.array([d_OMIB_i]))
191
d_r = sol.x
192
193
return d_r

==PAGE:262==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA
248
196
def InitialPdeltaParams(self,delta_OMIB,Pa_OMIB):
197
198
Pa1 = Pa_OMIB[0]
# at t = t_i-2t_stepsize
199
Pa2 = Pa_OMIB[1]
# at t = t_i-t_stepsize
200
Pa3 = Pa_OMIB[2]
# at t = t_i
201
d1 = delta_OMIB[0]
# at t = t_i-2t_stepsize
202
d2 = delta_OMIB[1]
# at t = t_i-t_stepsize
203
d3 = delta_OMIB[2]
# at t = t_i
204
205
# Three P-delta curves with successive measurements used to
solve for a,b,c:
206
207
a = ((-Pa2 + Pa3) * d1 + (Pa1 - Pa3) * d2 - d3 * (Pa1 - Pa2)) /
((d2 - d3) * (d1 - d3) * (d1 - d2))
208
b = ((Pa2 - Pa3) * d1 ** 2 + (-Pa1 + Pa3) * d2 ** 2 + d3 ** 2 *
(Pa1 - Pa2)) / ((d2 - d3) * (d1 - d3) * (d1 - d2))
209
c = ((-Pa2 * d3 + Pa3 * d2) * d1 ** 2 + (Pa2 * d3 ** 2 - Pa3 *
d2 ** 2) * d1 + d2 * d3 * Pa1 * (d2 - d3)) / ((d2 - d3) * (
d1 - d3) * (d1 - d2))
210
211
X = np.array([[d1 ** 2, d1, 1], [d2 ** 2, d2, 1], [d3 ** 2, d3,
1]])
212
213
R = np.transpose(X) @ X
214
try:
215
R_inv = np.linalg.inv(R)
216
except np.linalg.LinAlgError: # if the matrix is singular, then
try the psuedo inverse
217
R_inv = np.linalg.pinv(R)
218
219
Theta = np.array([[a], [b], [c]])
220
221
return R_inv, Theta
222
223
224
def RefinePdelta_RLS(self, d_OMIB_i, Pa_OMIB_i, R_inv_old, Theta_old
):
225
226
x_i = np.array([[d_OMIB_i ** 2], [d_OMIB_i], [1]], dtype=np.
float)
227
228
Lambda = 0.95
229
230
R_inv = 1 / Lambda * (R_inv_old - R_inv_old @ x_i * 1 / (Lambda
+ np.transpose(x_i) @ R_inv_old @ x_i) @ np.transpose(x_i) @
R_inv_old)
231
232
Theta = Theta_old + R_inv @ x_i @ (Pa_OMIB_i - np.transpose(x_i)
@ Theta_old)
233
234
return R_inv, Theta
235
==PAGE:263==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA
249

238
def rotor_angle_prediction(self,dt,time,delta,d_coi,
initializationOfParams):
239
240
if initializationOfParams:
241
t1 = time[0]
242
t2 = time[1]
243
t3 = time[2]
244
245
# obtaining the parameters for each quadratic equation for
each machine to predict delta
246
for i in range(len(self.A)):
247
248
d1 = delta[0, i]
249
d2 = delta[1, i]
250
d3 = delta[2, i]
251
252
self.A[i] = ((-d2 + d3) * t1 + (d1 - d3) * t2 - t3 * (d1
- d2)) / ((t2 - t3) * (t1 - t3) * (t1 - t2))
253
self.B[i] = ((d2 - d3) * t1 ** 2 + (-d1 + d3) * t2 ** 2
+ t3 ** 2 * (d1 - d2)) / ((t2 - t3) * (t1 - t3) * (
t1 - t2))
254
self.C[i] = ((-d2 * t3 + d3 * t2) * t1 ** 2 + (d2 * t3
** 2 - d3 * t2 ** 2) * t1 + d1 * t2 * t3 * (t2 - t3)
) / ((t2 - t3) * (t1 - t3) * (t1 - t2))
255
256
X_delta = np.array([[t1 ** 2, t1, 1], [t2 ** 2, t2, 1], [t3
** 2, t3, 1]])
257
R_delta = np.transpose(X_delta) @ X_delta
258
self.R_inv_delta = np.linalg.inv(R_delta)
259
self.Theta_delta = np.concatenate([[self.A], [self.B], [self
.C]])
260
261
t = round(time[-1] + dt, 4)
# s (t = t_i+dt)
262
263
else:
264
t_i = time
265
t = round(t_i + dt, 4)
266
delta_i = np.transpose(delta)
267
268
xd_i = np.array([[t_i ** 2], [t_i], [1]], dtype=np.float)
269
270
self.R_inv_delta_old = self.R_inv_delta
271
self.Theta_delta_old = self.Theta_delta
272
Lambda = 0.95
273
274
self.R_inv_delta = 1 / Lambda * (self.R_inv_delta_old - self
.R_inv_delta_old @ xd_i * 1 / (Lambda + np.transpose(
xd_i) @ self.R_inv_delta_old @ xd_i) @ np.transpose(xd_i
) @ self.R_inv_delta_old)
275
==PAGE:264==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA
250
276
self.Theta_delta = self.Theta_delta_old + self.R_inv_delta @
xd_i @ (delta_i - np.transpose(xd_i) @ self.
Theta_delta_old)
277
278
self.A = self.Theta_delta[0, :]
279
self.B = self.Theta_delta[1, :]
280
self.C = self.Theta_delta[2, :]
281
282
self.delta_future = self.A*t**2+self.B*t+self.C
283
284
self.delta_future = self.delta_future - d_coi
285
286
self.delta_future_sorted = np.sort(self.delta_future)[::-1]
287
288
self.map_loc_of_machs_in_delta_sorted = np.empty(0,dtype=np.int)
289
290
for i in range(len(self.delta_future_sorted)): # mapping from
delta_future_sorted to delta_future. Gives the location of
each generator in delta_future_sorted
291
self.map_loc_of_machs_in_delta_sorted = np.append(self.
map_loc_of_machs_in_delta_sorted , np.where(self.
delta_future_sorted[i] == self.delta_future)[0][0])
292
293
294
# this method is called right after the new rotor angles have been
predicted.
295
def SearchForNewClusters(self):
296
297
# calculating the rotor angle between two successive machines
298
for i in range(len(self.delta_future_sorted) - 1):
299
self.delta_gaps[i] = self.delta_future_sorted[i] - self.
delta_future_sorted[i + 1]
300
301
302
303
for ind,gap in enumerate([-1,-2,-3]): # finding the largest,
second and third largest gap, resulting in 3 possible OMIB
candidates per timestep
304
305
self.Angulargap = np.where(self.delta_gaps == np.sort(self.
delta_gaps)[gap])[0] # finding the index of either the
largest, second largest or third largest angular gap
306
307
CM_index = np.ones(len(self.gens),dtype=np.bool)*False
308
NM_index = np.ones(len(self.gens),dtype=np.bool)*False
309
310
CM_index[np.sort(self.map_loc_of_machs_in_delta_sorted[np.
arange(0, self.Angulargap + 1)])] = True
311
NM_index[np.sort(self.map_loc_of_machs_in_delta_sorted[np.
arange(self.Angulargap + 1, len(self.
map_loc_of_machs_in_delta_sorted))])] = True

==PAGE:265==
F.2 Implementation of TSA and TVSA

Angulargap are NMs.

self.numN = np.sum(NM_index)

(self.clusters[:,0] == self.numC): # checking if
this OMIB candidate already exists

([[self.numC, self.numN]])))

323
self.NM_index = np.vstack((self.NM_index,NM_index))

self.newNumCandidates += 1

'''
2
3
Computes the minimum voltage during the transient voltage sag
4
5
'''
6
